Al-Murdar on Dual Agency and Moral Responsibility
Al-Murdar's view on dual agency with distinct moral judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Murdar's claim that a single movement can be the act of both God and a human, and how he distinguishes moral responsibility for each agent.

Core Idea
One act, two agents—different moral judgments for each.

Abu Musa al-Murdar presents a nuanced view on divine and human agency. He argues that a single movement can be the act of both God and a human, yet each agent's moral responsibility differs. This challenges simpler conceptions of causality and ethics.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the key claim
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify al-Murdar's core claim that a movement is the act of both agents and an object of power for both.
Notice the term 'مقدورهما' (their maqdur) indicates equal capability.
2
Analyze the examples
The passage gives two examples: one with a commanded and forbidden agent, another with a human and the wind. Examine how al-Murdar assigns good or evil based on each agent's command status.
In the second example, the wind represents God's agency.
3
Understand the conclusion
Al-Murdar concludes that this view removes the disagreement with Ahl al-Haqq on the creation of acts. Reflect on how this reconciles divine causation with human responsibility.
The passage ends with 'على هذا لم يبق له في مسألة خلق الأفعال مع أهل الحق كلام'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم أبو موسى بن صبح المردار المعروف بناسك البغداديين أن الحركة فعلهما جميعًا ومقدورهما ولو كان أحد المحركين مأمورا به والآخر منهيًا عنه كانت الحركة طاعة من المأمور به حسنا، معصية من المنهي عنه قبيحًا، ولو كان أحد المحركين إنسانًا منهيًا عن التحريك والآخر الريح كانت الحركة فعل الله تعالى وفعل العبد، وهي معصية قبيحة من العبد، وهي حكمة من الصانع القديم جل وعلا، وكان القديم فعل ما هو معصية من غيره ولم يكن بذلك عاصيًا، وكذا لو كان الرجل مأمورًا بذلك كان الله تعالى فعل ما هو طاعة من العبد، ولم يكن بذلك مطيعا وعلى هذا لم يبق له في مسألة خلق الأفعال مع أهل الحق كلام.
And Abu Musa ibn Subh al-Murdar, known as the ascetic of the Baghdadis, claimed that the movement is the act of both of them and their maqdur. If one of the two movers is commanded to do it and the other is forbidden, the movement would be an act of obedience from the commanded one, good, and an act of disobedience from the forbidden one, evil. And if one of the two movers is a human forbidden to move and the other is the wind, the movement would be the act of God and the act of the servant, and it is an evil disobedience from the servant, and it is wisdom from the Eternal Maker, Exalted and Glorified. And the Eternal did what is disobedience from another, and He was not thereby disobedient. Likewise, if the man was commanded to do that, then God did what is an act of obedience from the servant, and He was not thereby obedient. On this basis, he has no further argument with Ahl al-Haqq on the issue of the creation of acts.
Source pages p. 910

Commentary
This passage presents the view of Abu Musa al-Murdar, a Mu'tazili scholar. He claims that a single movement can be the act of two agents simultaneously, and it is an object of power (maqdur) for both. He introduces a moral dimension: if one agent is commanded and the other forbidden, the same act can be good for the commanded one and evil for the forbidden one. For example, if a human is forbidden to move an object, but the wind (as an agent of God) moves it, the movement is from God and the human; it is evil for the human but wise from God. God can do what is disobedience from another without being disobedient Himself. The same applies to obedience. Al-Murdar concludes that on this basis, there is no further argument between him and Ahl al-Haqq (the Sunnis) on the issue of the creation of acts, since his view accommodates divine causation alongside human responsibility.

Key Terms
فعل (act)
مقدور (object of power)
طاعة (obedience)
معصية (disobedience)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that al-Murdar allows two agents to be equally morally responsible without distinction.
Correction: Al-Murdar assigns different moral verdicts based on each agent's command status, so they are not equally responsible.

Checkpoint
According to al-Murdar, if a human and the wind move an object, what is the moral status of the movement for the human?
A) It is good
B) It is evil
C) It is neutral
D) It is both good and evil

Key Takeaway
Al-Murdar maintains that a single act can have dual attribution and dual moral evaluation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever subtly blame God for creating the circumstance or your inclination? How does al-Murdar's insistence that the same act can be obedience from one and disobedience from another challenge you to take full ownership of your wrongdoing? What concrete step will you take today to repent and accept sole responsibility?
?
If God's wisdom is behind every movement He creates, even those that are sinful from you, does that change how you view trials and your own failures? Can you trust that God's wisdom is perfect even when your actions are evil? Write down one area of your life where you need to surrender your confusion about God's justice.
?
Al-Murdar says that God does what is disobedience from another without being disobedient. How does this distinction affect your understanding of God's holiness? Will you commit to never attributing evil to God, even when you don't understand His decree? What habit can you start to remind yourself of His perfect wisdom?

Three Options for the Opponent on Action and Agents
Al-Nasafi presents the opponent with three inevitable options after a logical dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Nasafi logically corners the opponent by presenting a dilemma with three inevitable choices.

Core Idea
The opponent must either accept Sunni doctrine, contradict his own school, or be branded obstinate.

Al-Nasafi presents a powerful logical refutation. He shows that if the Mu'tazilī denies that two agents can cooperate, then by the same logic, Allah cannot create equal agents. The opponent then has no rational escape, only three stark options.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the repeated structure: 'If you say... then say...'
2
Identify the dilemma and the three options
Find the dilemma: the opponent's own logic is turned against them. Then list the three options Al-Nasafi presents.
Option 1 is the Sunni position, option 2 contradicts Mu'tazilī doctrine, option 3 is obstinacy.
3
Reflect on the logical force
Consider why the opponent cannot escape. Why is there no fourth option? Discuss how this argument refutes the Mu'tazilī position on generated acts.
This argument is a classic example of ilzām (forcing a conclusion).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال له: إذا قلت يستحيل أن يتفق الاثنان على التحريك عند استوائهما في المعاني لأنه يؤدي إلى محال فقل يستحيل أن يخلق الله شخصين مستويي القدرة والبنية لأنه يؤدي إلى محال إذ لا فرق بينهما. ثم بعد ذلك إنك بين أمور ثلاثة: إما أن ترجع عن مسألة المتولدات وتجعلها الله تعالى كما هو مذهب أهل السنة، أو تجيز تعلق قدرتين بمقدور واحد وجعل شيء فعلاً لفاعلين وفيه رجوع عن مذهبك، أو تتمسك بالمذهبين مع لزوم الدليل فتوصف بالعناد المحض، والله الموفق.
Then it is said to him: When you say it is impossible for two to agree on moving when they are equal in attributes because it leads to absurdity, then say it is impossible for Allah to create two persons equal in power and constitution because it leads to absurdity, since there is no difference between them. Then after that, you are faced with three matters: Either you recant from the issue of generated acts (al-mutawallidāt) and attribute them to Allah Almighty as is the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunnah, or you permit the attachment of two powers to one act of power (maqdūr wāḥid) and make something an act of two agents — in which there is a recantation from your madhhab, or you adhere to both madhhabs while the proof necessitates [error] so you are described with pure obstinacy. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 910

Commentary
This passage is a logical refutation of the Mu'tazilī position. The author, al-Nasafī, begins by presenting a dilemma: if the opponent claims it is impossible for two agents to agree on a movement when they are equal in attributes because it leads to absurdity, then by the same logic, it would be impossible for Allah to create two persons equal in power and constitution. The opponent must then choose one of three options: (1) abandon the doctrine of generated acts (mutawallidāt) and attribute all actions to Allah, which is the Sunni view; (2) allow two powers to affect a single act, which contradicts his own school; or (3) stubbornly cling to both positions, which is pure obstinacy. The passage emphasizes that the opponent has no logical escape.

Key Terms
المتولدات (al-mutawallidāt)
قدرة (qudra)
مقدور (maqdūr)
العناد (obstinacy)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Al-Nasafi is allowing two agents to act on one thing.
Correction: Actually, he is presenting a consequence for the opponent to consider; it is not his own view.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi's argument, what is the first option for the opponent?
A) To recant from the issue of generated acts and attribute them to Allah
B) To permit two powers on one act
C) To adhere to both positions obstinately
D) To deny the possibility of equal agents

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi leaves the opponent with no rational choice but to either accept Sunni theology or contradict their own principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a clear logical proof that contradicts a belief you hold dear, do you have the courage to change your position, or do you find yourself clinging to it out of habit or pride? Which of the three options would you choose in that moment?
?
This lesson forces the opponent to confront the inconsistency between their principles and their practice. Where in your own life—your theology, your ethics, or your daily choices—are you holding contradictory positions without admitting it?
?
Al-Nasafi warns that clinging to both sides despite the proof is 'pure obstinacy.' Is there a belief you hold not because it is true, but because you are unwilling to admit you were wrong? Will you today ask Allah to show you the truth and grant you the humility to accept it, even if it costs you your comfort?

Al-Murdar's Doctrine of a Single Act from Two Agents
Al-Nasafi reports and critiques al-Murdar's view that a movement is the act of both agents simultaneously.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Murdar's controversial claim that a single act can be from two agents simultaneously and why al-Nasafi rejects it as logically and morally incoherent.

Core Idea
A single act cannot be the act of two agents without creating contradictory moral attributions.

Al-Nasafi presents and then refutes the view of al-Murdar, a Mu'tazili theologian who argued that a movement can be the act of both a human and Allah or two humans. This passage exposes the logical and moral absurdities that follow from such a position, ultimately showing why the Ash'ari/Maturidi view of exclusive divine creation of acts is more coherent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Note the technical terms: الحركة (movement), فعلهما (act of both), مقدورهما (object of power of both).
2
Identify al-Murdar's core claim
Find the sentence where al-Murdar states that a movement is the act and object of power of both agents together. Understand that this is a direct challenge to the Ash'ari view that acts are created exclusively by Allah.
3
Examine the first scenario
Al-Murdar gives an example: one agent is commanded, the other forbidden. The same movement is an obedience (good) from one and a disobedience (evil) from the other. Ask yourself: can the same act be both good and evil simultaneously?
4
Examine the second scenario
The second example involves a human and the wind. The movement is attributed to both Allah and the servant, but is disobedience from the servant and wisdom from Allah. This leads to the absurdity that Allah does what is disobedience from another without being disobedient.
5
Reflect on al-Nasafi's conclusion
Al-Nasafi concludes that al-Murdar has no argument left in the issue of the creation of acts against the people of truth. Consider why this refutation is effective.
The contradiction in moral attribution invalidates al-Murdar's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم أبو موسى بن صبح المردار المعروف بناسك البغداديين أن الحركة فعلهما جميعًا ومقدورهما ولو كان أحد المحركين مأمورا به والآخر منهيًا عنه كانت الحركة طاعة من المأمور به حسنا، معصية من المنهي عنه قبيحًا، ولو كان أحد المحركين إنسانًا منهيًا عن التحريك والآخر الريح كانت الحركة فعل الله تعالى وفعل العبد، وهي معصية قبيحة من العبد، وهي حكمة من الصانع القديم جل وعلا، وكان القديم فعل ما هو معصية من غيره ولم يكن بذلك عاصيًا، وكذا لو كان الرجل مأمورًا بذلك كان الله تعالى فعل ما هو طاعة من العبد، ولم يكن بذلك مطيعا وعلى هذا لم يبق له في مسألة خلق الأفعال مع أهل الحق كلام.
And Abū Mūsā ibn Ṣubḥ al-Murdār, known as the ascetic of the Baghdādīs, claimed that the movement is the act of both of them together and their object of power (maqdūr). If one of the two movers is commanded to do it and the other is forbidden from it, then the movement would be an obedience from the commanded one — good — and a disobedience from the forbidden one — evil. And if one of the two movers is a human forbidden from moving and the other is the wind, then the movement would be the act of Allāh Almighty and the act of the servant; it would be an evil disobedience from the servant, and it would be wisdom from the Eternal Maker, Exalted and High. The Eternal did what is disobedience from another without thereby being disobedient. Similarly, if the man were commanded to do that, then Allāh would have done what is obedience from the servant without being obedient by it. And on this, he has no argument left in the issue of the creation of acts against the people of truth.
Source pages p. 910

Commentary
This passage reports the view of al-Murdār, a Mu'tazilī theologian. He claimed that a single movement can be the act and object of power of two agents simultaneously. Al-Nasafī exposes the consequence: if one agent is commanded and the other forbidden, the same act is both good (from one perspective) and evil (from another). This leads to absurdities such as attributing disobedience to the servant while the act is also from Allāh, yet Allāh is not disobedient. Al-Murdār's position fails to provide a consistent account, and al-Nasafī concludes that he has no valid argument against the Ahl al-Ḥaqq (people of truth).

Key Terms
الحركة (al-ḥaraka)
فعلهما (act of both)
مقدورهما (object of power of both)
طاعة (obedience)
معصية (disobedience)
خالق الأفعال (Creator of acts)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Murdār's view solves the problem of human agency by allowing acts to be from both humans and Allah.
Correction: It creates contradictory moral attributions: the same act would be both good and evil, and Allah would be the doer of disobedience from another without being disobedient, which is incoherent.

Checkpoint
What does al-Murdār claim about a movement?
A) It is the act of one agent only
B) It is the act of both agents together
C) It is uncreated
D) It is not a real act

Key Takeaway
Al-Murdār's attempt to attribute a single act to two agents leads to contradictory moral judgments and ultimately fails, leaving him no argument against the orthodox view that Allah alone creates acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Murdār tried to share responsibility for an act between Allah and the servant. When you sin, do you ever subtly shift part of the blame to circumstances or even to Allah's decree? How does this lesson expose that as a contradiction in your own soul?
?
The same act cannot be both obedience and disobedience. In your life, where are you holding double standards—calling your own actions 'necessary' or 'justified' while condemning others for the same? Will you repent from that today?
?
If you truly believed that Allah alone creates every act, would you still make excuses for your sins? What would change in how you seek forgiveness tonight?

Abu Hudhayl's Attempt and Al-Nasafi's Refutation
Al-Nasafi refutes Abu Hudhayl's theory of divided agency via a three-pronged dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Abu Hudhayl's attempt to avoid al-Murdar's problem by dividing movement by agents fails, and how Al-Nasafi uses a logical dilemma to expose its inconsistency.

Core Idea
Dividing an indivisible act by agents leads to the same absurdities, not a solution.

Al-Nasafi continues his refutation of Mu'tazili views on divine and human agency. Abu Hudhayl al-'Allaf tried to refine the position by claiming that a single movement, though indivisible in itself, can be divided by its agents and aspects. Yet this move, as Al-Nasafi shows, only postpones the problem. This lesson traces Al-Nasafi's logical dilemma that leaves no escape for Abu Hudhayl.

Guided Path
1
Identify Abu Hudhayl's claim
Read the first sentence of the passage. Note how Abu Hudhayl distinguishes between division in essence and division by agents. Understand that he is trying to avoid al-Murdar's problem.
This is a key Mu'tazili attempt to reconcile divine omnipotence with human agency.
2
Grasp the immediate objection
Al-Nasafi points out that the same problem remains: the act would be obedience and disobedience based on the agent. This shows the attempted refinement fails.
3
Examine the key question
Al-Nasafi asks: when the movement is divided over agents, is the portion of each agent the same or different? This question sets up a dilemma with three possible answers.
Each answer leads to a contradiction or an unwanted consequence.
4
Trace each answer and its refutation
Work through the three answers: (1) same portion, (2) different expression but same reality, (3) truly different. Notice how each is shown to be inconsistent with Abu Hudhayl's own principles.
The third answer forces acceptance of the divisibility of accidents, which Abu Hudhayl rejects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم أبو الهذيل العلاف أن حركة كل جزء من أجزاء الحجر مقسمة على كل واحد منهما لا في نفسها لأنها حركة واحدة وشيء واحد، والأعراض لا تنقسم في ذاتها، وإنما تنقسم بالمكان والزمان والفاعلين. ويلزمه جميع ما لزم المردار إذا كانت هي باعتبار ذاتها غير منقسمة فكانت هي باعتبار أحد الفاعلين طاعة وبالاعتبار الآخر معصية، وكذا إذا كان أحد المحركين ريحًا إذ هي في نفسها غير منقسمة فيلزمه جميع ما ألزمنا، ولم ينفعه هذا التجاهل. ثم نبين تجاهله فنقول له: إذا انقسمت الحركة على الفاعلين أكان قسم كل واحد منهما هو قسم صاحبه بعينه لا غير أم هو غير قسم صاحبه؟ فإن قال: هو قسم صاحبه، فإذا كان الفعل لهما لا على الانقسام، وكان على ما عليه قول خصومه من كونه طاعة ومعصية وفعلاً لفاعلين ومقدوراً لقادرين ولم يحصل الفرق بين انقسامها وامتناعها عن الانقسام، إذ لو قيل هي لا تنقسم لم يكن تحت هذا القول إلا هذا أن قسم كل واحد منهما هو قسم صاحبه. وإن قال: قسم كل واحد منهما غير قسم صاحبه في الذكر والعبارة إلا أن المنقسم واحد في الحقيقة. قيل: وخصومك يقولون: الكسب غير الخلق، وما عذب عليه غير ما لم يعذب عليه، وما قدر عليه الإنسان من الاكتساب غير ما لم يقدر عليه من الخلق في العبارة، والمخلوق هو المكتسب في الحقيقة، على هذا كان يتكلم حفص، فإن كان قولك هذا صحيحًا فقولهم كان صحيحا، وإن كان قولهم باطلاً كان قولك باطلاً. وإن أن أحد القسمين غير صاحبه على الحقيقة فهذا هو القول بتجزؤ الأعراض زعم حرب: وانقسامها وهو لا يقول به، بل هو قول جعفر بن حرب ويقال له أيضا ما
And Abū al-Hudhayl al-ʿAllāf claimed that the movement of every part of the stone is divided over each one of them, but not in itself, because it is one movement and one thing, and accidents (aʿrāḍ) are not divided in themselves; they are only divided by place, time, and agents. There follows for him all that followed for al-Murdār, since it is, in terms of its essence, indivisible. So it would be, in terms of one agent, obedience, and in terms of the other, disobedience. Similarly, if one of the movers is wind, since it is in itself indivisible, then all that we obligated follows for him, and this feigned ignorance does not benefit him. Then we expose his feigned ignorance and say to him: If the movement is divided over the agents, is the portion of each one the exact portion of its companion or is it other than the portion of its companion? If he says: It is the portion of its companion, then the act is from them both without division, and it would be as the opponents say: it is obedience and disobedience, an act of two agents, and an object of power for two powerful ones. There is no difference between its division and its refusal of division, because if it were said it is not divided, this statement would only mean that the portion of each is the portion of its companion. And if he says: The portion of each is other than the portion of its companion in mention and expression, but the divided one is one in reality, it is said: And your opponents say: Acquisition (al-kasb) is other than creation (al-khalq), and what is punished for is other than what is not punished for, and what man is capable of in terms of acquisition is other than what he is not capable of in terms of creation in expression, while the created is the acquired in reality. On this Ḥafṣ used to speak. If your statement is correct, then their statement is correct; and if their statement is false, then your statement is false. And if one of the two portions is other than its companion in reality, then this is the doctrine of the divisibility of accidents, claimed by Ḥarb, and his division, but he does not hold it; rather it is the doctrine of Jaʿfar ibn Ḥarb, and it is also said to him...
Source pages p. 910

Commentary
This passage first reports the view of Abū al-Hudhayl al-ʿAllāf, a prominent Muʿtazilī. He attempted to avoid al-Murdār's problem by claiming that a movement, though one indivisible accident, can be divided by agents and aspects. Al-Nasafī points out that this still leads to the same consequence: the same act would be both obedience and disobedience, etc. He then launches a refutation by asking a key question: when the movement is divided over the agents, is the portion of each the same portion as the other's or different? If the same, then the act is from both without real division, and the opponent's view collapses into the same problems. If different in expression but one in reality, then the opponent's distinction between kasb and khalq (acquisition vs. creation) is analogous, but if that is false, so is his view. If truly different, then one must accept the divisibility of accidents, which the opponent rejects, but that is the view of Jaʿfar ibn Ḥarb. The argument effectively leaves the opponent no consistent position.

Key Terms
الأعراض (aʿrāḍ)
انقسام (division)
الكسب (al-kasb)
الخلق (al-khalq)
حفص (Ḥafṣ)
جعفر بن حرب (Jaʿfar ibn Ḥarb)
الحركة (movement)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abu Hudhayl's division by agents solves the problem of a single act having contradictory moral qualities.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that this division merely shifts the absurdity: either the portions are the same (leading to the same problem) or different (leading to other contradictions).

Checkpoint
What is the key question al-Nasafī asks Abū al-Hudhayl?
A) Whether movement is an accident
B) Whether the portion of each agent is the same or different
C) Whether Allah creates all acts
D) Whether acts can be good and evil

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi's dilemma shows that any attempt to attribute a single act to two agents while preserving its unity leads to logical inconsistency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological difficulty, do you ever cling to a comfortable illusion rather than face the unsettling implications of your beliefs? This lesson shows even a great scholar's evasion was exposed. Where in your own faith are you avoiding a hard truth? Will you ask Allah to show you the reality, even if it disturbs you?
?
Al-Nasafi's dilemma forces a choice between three unpalatable options. Are there contradictions in your life that you try to reconcile with superficial reasoning instead of humbly admitting you don't know? Take a moment to identify one such area and commit to seeking clarity through sincere study and duʿā.
?
This passage refutes a human attempt to divide an indivisible act between God and creation. Do you ever try to parcel out your life into 'God's part' and 'your part', as if you could neatly separate divine sovereignty from human responsibility? Could it be that you are holding back some area from total submission? What is one step you can take today to surrender that domain fully to Allah?

Mu'tazilite Objection to Kasb as Unintelligible
The Mu'tazila assert that kasb is unintelligible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite objection that the Ash'ari/Māturīdī doctrine of kasb is rationally unintelligible, and why this sets the stage for a defense.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila argue that kasb is an irrational concept.

The Ash'ari and Māturīdī schools maintain that human actions involve a divine creation (khalq) and human acquisition (kasb). The Mu'tazila, however, reject this distinction as incoherent. This brief passage records their core objection: kasb is 'unintelligible' (ghayr ma'qūl). Understanding this challenge is essential to grasping the subsequent defense and the entire debate over human agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic sentence and its literal translation. Notice the concise style and the key term 'غير معقول'.
This is a one-sentence record of the objection.
2
Identify the subject and claim
Identify that the subject is the Mu'tazila and their claim is that 'what you claim of kasb is unintelligible'.
The 'you' refers to the Ash'ari/Māturīdī theologians.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why calling a concept 'unintelligible' is a serious rational challenge. This objection opens the door for a defense of kasb's coherence.
The author will likely refute this claim in the following pages.

This brief passage records the Mu'tazilite objection to the Ash'ari/Māturīdī doctrine of kasb. The text cuts off here, so the author's response is not included in this passage. The footnote (not part of the main mush) mentions 'Abd al-Jabbār al-Rāzī's definition and the view of Abu Hashim. The objection sets the stage for a refutation likely in the following pages.

The Meaning of Kasb and Terminological Agreement
Nasafi defines kasb linguistically and shows agreement with Mu'tazila in meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Ahl al-Sunna and Mu'tazila agree on the reality of human action, differing only in naming it as 'kasb' or 'khalq'.

Core Idea
The dispute is over words, not deeds — both sides affirm the servant acts.

Nasafi begins by clarifying the linguistic meaning of 'muktasab' and 'iktisab', then responds to the Mu'tazilite Abd al-Jabbar who accused the Ahl al-Sunna of inconsistency. The author shows that the disagreement is merely terminological: what the Mu'tazila call 'creation', the Ahl al-Sunna call 'acquisition'. This lesson explores that clarification.

Guided Path
1
Read the linguistic definitions
Read the opening sentences where Nasafi defines 'muktasab' and distinguishes between actions that are acquired and those that are not.
Notice that the first action of the servant is not called 'acquired'.
2
Understand the Jabriyya accusation
Abd al-Jabbar accuses the Jabriyya (Ahl al-Sunna) of contradiction in saying the servant acquires but does not act.
He argues this contradicts their own religious duties.
3
Recognize the terminological agreement
Nasafi responds that the difference is only in expression: they call it khalq and ikhtira', we call it kasb. Both sides agree that the servant acts.
The deeper difference lies in attribution of power, which will be explored later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمكتسب هو المال ولذلك لا يوصف تعالى بالاكتساب وقد يكون من فعل العبد ما هو مكتسب إذا كان خيرًا أو شرًا اجتلبه بغيره من الأفعال، فأما أول أفعاله فلا يقال فيه إنه مكتسب وإنما يسمى اكتسابًا وقد يكون في أفعاله ما لا يكون اكتسابًا إذا لم يكتسب به نفعًا أو ضرا كحركات الطفل والنائم والساهي.. وكل هذا يبين من جهة اللغة أن المكتسب لابد من أن يكون فاعلاً ومحدثًا. ويقول عبد الجبار الرازي: وكل هذا يبين تجاهل المجبرة في قولهم: إن العبد يكتسب ولا يفعل، وإن هذا ينقض سائر ما يتعلقون به في باب العبادة عند فصلهم بين الكسب والفعل وكأنهم تجنبوا القول بأن الإنسان يفعل. فإذا قلنا بمثل ما قالوا ولم يبق بيننا خلاف إلا في العبارة فإنهم سموا ذلك خلقًا واختراعا، ونحن سميناه كسبًا إلا أن ما وراء ذلك عندنا متعلق بقدرة الله تعالى وعندهم لا يقدره أحد فاستوينا في جنبة العبد ولم يبق لهم علينا إشكال، وفيما وراء ذلك التحقوا بالدهرية والمعطلة، ونحن ثبنا على القول بثبوت الصانع بل هم أتوا بما هو غير المعقول وأبطلوا القول بتعلق القدرة بالمقدور، فإنهم أحالوا تعلقها إلا بالوجود والوجود راجع إلى الذات إذ هو ليس بمعنى وراء الذات ولا تعلق لها بالذات والشيئية فإن تعلقها بما لا تعلق لها به وهو محال وهذيان وبطل بهذا الفصل جميع قواعد المعتزلة وأضمحلت شبهاتهم، ونحن نحمد الله في غاية الوضوح.
The acquired (muktasab) is property, and therefore the Exalted is not described with acquisition (iktisab). And from the action of the servant there may be what is acquired if it is good or evil which he brings about by means of other actions. As for his first action, it is not said that it is acquired, but rather it is called acquisition (iktisab). And there may be among his actions what is not acquisition if he does not acquire benefit or harm through it, like the movements of a child, a sleeper, or one inattentive. And all this shows from a linguistic perspective that the acquirer must be an agent (fa'il) and an originator (muhdith). And Abd al-Jabbar al-Razi says: All this shows the ignorance of the Jabriyya in their saying that the servant acquires but does not act, and that this contradicts everything they hold in the chapter of worship when they distinguish between kasb and action, as if they avoided saying that man acts. So if we say the like of what they said, and no difference remains between us except in expression—for they called that creation (khalq) and invention (ikhtira'), while we called it acquisition (kasb)—except that beyond that, according to us, it is attached to the power of God Almighty, while according to them no one has power over it. So we are equal in the aspect of the servant, and no objection remains against us. And regarding what is beyond that, they have joined the Dahriyya and the Mu'attila. And we have affirmed the statement establishing the Maker; indeed they have brought what is irrational and nullified the statement that power attaches to the object of power (maqdur). For they made its attachment only to existence, and existence refers back to the essence (dhat) since it is not a meaning additional to the essence, and power has no attachment to the essence and to thingness (shay'iyya). So its attachment to what it has no attachment to is impossible and nonsense, and by this section all the principles of the Mu'tazila are invalidated and their doubts are dissipated. And we praise God for the utmost clarity.
Source pages p. 919

Commentary
This passage addresses the Mu'tazilite objection regarding the concept of kasb (acquisition). The author begins by clarifying the linguistic meaning of 'muktasab' (acquired), stating that it refers to property, and that God is not described with acquisition. He explains that some human actions are called 'acquired' if they are obtained through other actions, but the first action is not so called. Actions done without intention (like child's movement) are not acquisitions. Linguistically, the acquirer must be an agent and originator. Then he quotes Abd al-Jabbar al-Razi, who accuses the Jabriyya (a label for the Ahl al-Sunna) of inconsistency: they say the servant acquires but does not act, and this contradicts their own religious duties since they distinguish between kasb and action. The author responds by stating that in reality there is no difference except in terminology: the Mu'tazila call it 'creation' and 'invention', while the Ahl al-Sunna call it 'acquisition'. The core issue is what lies beyond the servant's power: for the Ahl al-Sunna, that is attached to God's power; for the Mu'tazila, no one has power over it. This reduces the Mu'tazila to the position of the Dahriyya (materialists) and the Mu'attila (those who deny attributes). The author concludes that the Mu'tazila have undermined their own position about power, which leads to absurdity.

Key Terms
الكسب
الاختراع
المكتسب
الفاعل
المحدث
المجبرة
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ahl al-Sunna believe humans do not truly act; they are merely passive recipients.
Correction: The passage shows that the acquirer must be an agent and originator, so the Ahl al-Sunna affirm human action.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the fundamental difference between the Ahl al-Sunna and the Mu'tazila regarding the actions of the servant?
A) The Ahl al-Sunna say the servant creates his actions, the Mu'tazila say God creates them
B) They differ only in terminology: the Mu'tazila call it 'creation', the Ahl al-Sunna call it 'acquisition'
C) The Mu'tazila affirm the servant's free will while the Ahl al-Sunna deny it
D) They agree entirely but the Mu'tazila add a condition of power

Key Takeaway
The Ahl al-Sunna and Mu'tazila agree that the servant acts, differing only in calling it 'kasb' or 'khalq'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you truly feel it is your action, yet entirely sustained by Allah? Or is the kasb doctrine just a mental concept that doesn't move your heart? Examine your prayer right now—are you present as an agent before Allah, or merely going through motions?
?
The passage shows that even the Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna agree on the reality of human action, differing only in words. Where in your life do you argue about labels (e.g., 'Sufi', 'Salafi') while missing the shared reality of servitude to Allah? Commit to one act today where you prioritize substance over terminology.
?
Nasafi uses the opponent's own argument to show agreement. Think of a person you disagree with religiously. Can you find common ground in their own statements? What would it require of your pride to acknowledge that agreement? Write down one concrete step to build unity despite differences.

The Deeper Implications: Mu'tazilite Power Theory and Incoherence
Nasafi critiques Mu'tazilite theory of power and its incoherence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazilite position on power leads to irrational conclusions and aligns them with materialists.

Core Idea
Denying God's power over human actions leads to absurdity.

After showing the terminological agreement, Nasafi turns to the critical difference: for the Ahl al-Sunna, the ultimate power over the action belongs to God, while the Mu'tazila deny that anyone has power over it. This, the author argues, leads the Mu'tazila to join the Dahriyya and Mu'attila. In this lesson, we explore that critique and its consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the difference in attribution of power
Read the first sentence to see that for the Ahl al-Sunna, beyond the servant's action it is attached to God's power, while the Mu'tazila say no one has power over it.
This is the key divergence.
2
Understand the accusation of joining the Dahriyya
Note how Nasafi claims that by denying any power over human actions, the Mu'tazila align with materialists (Dahriyya) and those who deny attributes (Mu'attila).
This is a strong accusation.
3
Analyze the critique of Mu'tazilite power theory
Read the argument that they limited power's attachment only to existence, which reduces to essence, making the attachment impossible and absurd.
Nasafi concludes that this invalidates all their principles.

Nasafi's Refutation of Mu'tazilite 'Ikhtira''
Nasafi queries Mu'tazilite meaning of 'ikhtira'' and refutes four possible definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that human power is power over invention by exposing the inherent contradictions in the term 'ikhtira''.

Core Idea
The term 'ikhtira'' is either impossible, contradictory, or meaningless when examined logically.

Nasafi now engages with a key Mu'tazilite claim: that human power is power over 'ikhtira'' (invention). He asks what 'ikhtira'' actually means and systematically shows that all possible answers lead to absurdity. This passage demonstrates the Maturidi method of logical refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that this is a tight logical argument, not a simple statement.
2
Identify the four possibilities
The passage presents four possible meanings for 'ikhtira''. List them in order: existent, non-existent, both, neither.
Each possibility corresponds to a different interpretation of the term.
3
Follow each refutation
For each possibility, note the specific reason Nasafi gives for why it fails. Connect each refutation to Mu'tazilite principles.
The refutations rely on Mu'tazilite views about power attaching to existents and attributes of non-existents.
4
Synthesize the overall conclusion
After working through all four, ask yourself: What is the overall point? How does this undermine the Mu'tazilite position on human power?
The term 'ikhtira'' is either impossible, contradictory, or meaningless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول ما معنى قولكم: إن قدرتنا قدرة على الاختراع؟ وما هذا الاختراع الذي تزعمون أن قدرتكم متعلقة به أهو اسم لموجود أم لمعدوم وموجود أم لا لموجود ولا لمعدوم؟ فإن قلتم: إنه اسم لموجود، فهو محال عندكم لأن تعلق القدرة بالموجود عندكم محال، وإن قلتم: إنه اسم لمعدوم، فهو محال أيضا، لأنه يوجب أن يكون في العدم اختراع وكل وصف كان في العدم ثابتا كان تعلق القدرة به محالاً كالشيئية والجوهر والعرضية والعينية وغير ذلك من الأوصاف الثابتة في العدم عندكم، وإن قلتم إنه اسم للموجود والمعدوم فهذا كلام متناقض، وإن قلتم إنه اسم لا لموجود ولا لمعدوم فهو اسم لما لا يعقل إذ لا يتصور معلوم ليس بموجود ولا معدوم، وهذا.
Then we say: What is the meaning of your statement that our power is power over invention (ikhtira')? And what is this invention that you claim your power is attached to? Is it a name for an existent or for a non-existent, or for both existent and non-existent, or for neither existent nor non-existent? For if you say it is a name for an existent, that is impossible according to you because the attachment of power to an existent is impossible in your view. And if you say it is a name for a non-existent, that is also impossible, because it would require that there is invention in non-existence, and every attribute that is established in non-existence, the attachment of power to it is impossible, like thingness (shay'iyya), substance (jawhar), accidentality (aradiyya), entity-ness (ayniyya), and other attributes that are established in non-existence according to you. And if you say it is a name for both existent and non-existent, that is a contradictory statement. And if you say it is a name for neither existent nor non-existent, then it is a name for what is not rational, since no known thing can be conceived that is neither existent nor non-existent. And this.
Source pages p. 920

Commentary
This passage presents a logical objection to the Mu'tazilite claim that human power is power over 'ikhtira'' (invention). The author asks for clarification: what does 'ikhtira'' denote? He systematically lists four possibilities and refutes each based on Mu'tazilite principles. First, if 'ikhtira'' refers to an existent, that is impossible because the Mu'tazila themselves hold that power cannot attach to an already existent thing (since it is already actual). Second, if it refers to a non-existent, that is also impossible because the Mu'tazila hold that non-existents have fixed attributes (like thingness, substance, etc.), and power cannot attach to those. Third, if it refers to both existent and non-existent, that is self-contradictory. Fourth, if it refers to neither, then it is a meaningless term with no rational content, since every known thing is either existent or non-existent. Thus, the term 'ikhtira'' is either impossible, contradictory, or meaningless, demonstrating the incoherence of the Mu'tazilite claim that human power is power over invention.

Key Terms
الاختراع
الموجود
المعدوم
القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilite term 'ikhtira'' is a clear and meaningful concept that can be defined consistently.
Correction: Nasafi shows that any attempt to define 'ikhtira'' leads to impossibility, contradiction, or meaninglessness, thus the term is incoherent.

Checkpoint
What is the fourth possibility for the meaning of 'ikhtira'' that the author refutes?
A) It is a name for an existent
B) It is a name for a non-existent
C) It is a name for both existent and non-existent
D) It is a name for neither existent nor non-existent

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite concept of 'ikhtira'' is logically indefensible because all possible definitions lead to contradiction or absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you held a belief that crumbled under logical examination because you couldn't define its core terms? What did you do when you faced that inconsistency—did you cling to the term or abandon it?
?
The Mu'tazilites claimed human power produces 'invention,' but their own principles made the term incoherent. Are there any concepts in your own understanding of faith that you have accepted without defining clearly? Which one are you most afraid to examine logically?
?
Nasafi shows that a term can sound meaningful but be empty when pushed. Are you willing today to take one doctrine you believe and test it against the same standard: can it survive a rigorous analysis of its key terms? Commit to doing that this week.

intermediate
5 min
The Incoherence of 'Ikhtira'' (Invention)
The author poses a dilemma about the term 'ikhtira'' and refutes each possible definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazili concept of 'invention' (ikhtira') is incoherent when examined through its possible definitions.

Core Idea
The term 'invention' cannot be defined without contradiction or absurdity.

The Mu'tazila claim that human power is a power over 'invention' (ikhtira'), but what exactly does that mean? The author systematically examines every possible definition—existent, non-existent, both, or neither—and shows that each leads to an impossibility or contradiction. This passage demonstrates the logical rigor of Sunni kalam in exposing incoherent concepts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to understand the flow of the argument.
Notice how the author presents a dilemma with four branches.
2
Identify the four possibilities
The passage lists four possible meanings for 'ikhtira': existent, non-existent, both, or neither. Identify each in the text.
These are the four horns of the dilemma.
3
Follow the refutation of each
For each definition, the author gives a reason why it is impossible or contradictory. Trace the reasoning for each.
The refutations rely on Mu'tazili own principles.
4
Understand the comparison to Batinites
The last option is compared to the Batinite claim about Allah, showing that it leads to atheism and sophistry.
This exposes the danger of undefined terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول ما معنى قولكم: إن قدرتنا قدرة على الاختراع؟ وما هذا الاختراع الذي تزعمون أن قدرتكم متعلقة به أهو اسم لموجود أم لمعدوم أم لا لموجود ولا لمعدوم؟. فإن قلتم : إنه اسم لموجود، فهو محال عندكم لأن تعلق القدرة بالموجود عندكم محال، وإن قلتم : إنه اسم لمعدوم، فهو محال أيضا، لأنه يوجب أن يكون في العدم اختراع وكل وصف كان في العدم ثابتا كان تعلق القدرة به محالاً كالشيئية والجوهر والعرضية والعينية وغير ذلك من الأوصاف الثابتة في العدم عندكم، وإن قلتم إنه اسم للموجود والمعدوم فهذا كلام متناقض، وإن قلتم إنه اسم لا لموجود ولا لمعدوم فهو اسم لما لا يعقل إذ لا يتصور معلوم ليس بموجود ولا معدوم، وهذا من جهالات الباطنية فإنهم يزعمون أن الباري عز وجل ليس بموجود ولا معدوم، وهذا قول يؤدي إلى الإلحاد والسفسطة، والله الموفق.
Then we say: What is the meaning of your statement: 'Our power is a power over invention'? And what is this 'invention' to which you claim your power is attached—is it a name for an existent, or for a non-existent, or for neither existent nor non-existent? If you say it is a name for an existent, that is impossible according to you because attachment of power to the existent is impossible in your view. And if you say it is a name for a non-existent, that is also impossible, because it would require that there be invention in non-existence, and every attribute that is established in non-existence makes attachment of power to it impossible—like thingness, substance, accidentness, essence-ness, and other attributes established in non-existence according to you. And if you say it is a name for both existent and non-existent, that is contradictory speech. And if you say it is a name for neither existent nor non-existent, then it is a name for what is unintelligible, since no known thing can be conceived that is neither existent nor non-existent. This is part of the ignorances of the Batinites, for they claim that the Exalted Creator is neither existent nor non-existent, and this is a saying that leads to atheism and sophistry. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 920

Commentary
This passage is a classification and refutation of possible definitions of the Mu'tazili term 'invention' (ikhtira'). The author presents a fourfold dilemma. First, if it is a name for an existent, that is impossible because the Mu'tazila themselves hold that power cannot attach to what already exists. Second, if it is a name for a non-existent, that is also impossible because attributes like 'thingness' are established in non-existence, and power cannot attach to such fixed attributes. Third, if it is a name for both existent and non-existent, that is self-contradictory. Fourth, if it is a name for neither, then it refers to something inconceivable, since everything known is either existent or non-existent. The author then compares this last position to the Batinites (esoteric sect) who absurdly describe God as neither existent nor non-existent, leading to atheism and sophistry.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'invention' (ikhtira') is a meaningful concept that can be coherently defined within Mu'tazili theology.
Correction: The author shows that every possible definition of 'invention' leads to either impossibility, contradiction, or unintelligibility, thus the concept is incoherent.

Checkpoint
What happens if the Mu'tazila define 'ikhtira'' as a name for a non-existent?
A) It is acceptable because non-existence can be invented.
B) It is impossible because it would require power to attach to attributes fixed in non-existence.
C) It is the same as the Ash'ari position.
D) It leads to atheism.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili notion of 'invention' collapses because every attempt to define it leads to absurdity or contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author ties an unclear definition to the path of atheism. Are there any areas of your faith where you have accepted convenient but undefined ideas? Will you commit to seeking clarity before it leads you astray?
?
The Batinites claimed Allah is neither existent nor non-existent—a position of evasion. Are there issues in your own understanding of Allah's attributes where you have settled for ambiguity to avoid uncomfortable truths? Today, resolve to either affirm or reject a clear position.

Refuting the Batiniyya on the Existence of the Creator
Refutes the Batiniyya claim about the Creator's existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Batiniyya's claim that the Creator is neither existent nor non-existent is considered foolish and leads to atheism and sophistry.

Core Idea
Denying existence and non-existence for the Creator is atheism in disguise.

The Batiniyya (esotericist sect) made an extreme claim about the Creator that seems philosophical but is actually a form of sophistry. In this brief passage, Imam al-Nasafi identifies their error and warns that it leads to atheism. Let's examine what the claim is and why it is so dangerous.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how the author frames the claim as 'foolishness' from the start.
The Arabic word 'جهالات' is plural, emphasizing multiple foolish acts.
2
Understand the Batiniyya claim
Reflect on the claim: the Creator is neither existent nor non-existent. Why is this logically impossible? The principle of excluded middle says everything must be one or the other.
The author does not explain why it's impossible here; he simply condemns it.
3
Note the consequences
Imam al-Nasafi states that this claim leads to al-ilhad (atheism/heresy) and al-safsata (sophistry). He ends with a pious invocation, sealing the judgment.
The phrase 'والله الموفق' is a common closing, asking Allah for success.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من جهالات الباطنية فإنهم يزعمون أن الباري عز وجل ليس بموجود ولا معدوم، وهذا قول يؤدي إلى الإلحاد والسفسطة، والله الموفق.
From the foolishness of the Batiniyya is that they claim that the Creator, may He be exalted, is not existent and not non-existent, and this statement leads to atheism and sophistry, and God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 921

Commentary
This passage defines a specific error of the Batiniyya (esotericist sect). They claim that God is neither existent nor non-existent, which contradicts the rational truth that everything is either existent or non-existent. The author declares this claim to be foolishness and states that it leads to ilhad (atheism/heresy) and safsata (sophistry). He ends with the phrase 'and God is the grantor of success' as a pious invocation.

Key Terms

الإلحاد
السفسطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the claim that the Creator is neither existent nor non-existent is a form of extreme tanzih (transcendence), removing Him from both categories.
Correction: The author shows it leads to atheism and sophistry, not tanzih. It is a logical contradiction, not a valid theological nuance.

Checkpoint

B) Iman and tawheed
C) Atheism and sophistry
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
The Batiniyya's claim that the Creator is neither existent nor non-existent is a sophistical falsehood that leads to atheism, not a valid theological position.

Refutation of Abu Hudhayl's Compulsion in Paradise
Nasafi exposes the absurd consequences of Abu Hudhayl's view of compulsion in Paradise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Abu Hudhayl's view on compulsion in Paradise leads to an absurd theological dilemma.

Core Idea
Compelling actions in Paradise would make Allah the doer of human acts.

In this passage, Nasafi takes on the view of Abu Hudhayl, a Mu'tazili leader who held that the people of Paradise are compelled in their actions. Nasafi shows that this view leads to an impossible conclusion, forcing Abu Hudhayl into a dilemma. Let's read the passage and see how Nasafi's logical argument unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully. Then read the literal translation. Try to grasp the overall argument without overanalyzing.
Notice the list of actions: eating, drinking, sexual intercourse, walking, sitting, grasping, spreading.
2
Identify the logical conclusion
Nasafi argues that if all actions are compelled and directly created by Allah, then the doer of those actions must be Allah Himself. This is the key implication.
The phrase 'فيكون على قود كلامه هذا' indicates the logical consequence.
3
Understand the dilemma
Nasafi presents a dilemma: either Abu Hudhayl accepts that Allah performs all these actions (which is kufr) or he abandons his argument. See how this forces a choice.
The dilemma is in the final sentence: 'فكان هو بين أمرين إما أن يلتزم ذلك كله فيكفر، وإما أن يمتنع فيبطل حجاجه'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من مذهب رئيسكم أبي الهذيل أن أهل الجنة مجبورون على أفعالهم وما يوجد منهم من الأكل والشرب والجماع والمشي والقعود والقبض والبسط كله فعل الله تعالى بلا اختيار للعبد حتى قيل: إن أبا الهذيل جهمي الآخرة فيكون على قود كلامه هذا الآكل الشارب المجامع الماشي القاعد القابض الباسط هو الله تعالى الله عما يقولون علوا كبيرًا فكان هو بين أمرين إما أن يلتزم ذلك كله فيكفر، وإما أن يمتنع فيبطل حجاجه.
Then from the doctrine of your leader Abu Hudhayl is that the people of Paradise are compelled in their actions, and what appears from them of eating, drinking, sexual intercourse, walking, sitting, grasping, and spreading is all an act of Allah without any choice for the servant, until it was said: 'Indeed Abu Hudhayl is a Jahmi of the Hereafter.' So according to the implication of his statement, the eater, drinker, copulater, walker, sitter, grasper, spreader would be Allah Himself—exalted is Allah above what they say, greatly high. So he was between two matters: either he accepts all that and thus disbelieves, or he refrains and thus his argument is invalidated.
Source pages p. 923

Commentary
This passage refutes Abu Hudhayl al-Allaf's position by reductio ad absurdum. Abu Hudhayl held that the people of Paradise have no choice in their actions (jabr), and all their actions are directly created by Allah. Nasafi argues that this logically entails that Allah Himself is the one who eats, drinks, has intercourse, etc. in Paradise, which is impossible. Thus Abu Hudhayl faces a dilemma: either he accepts this blasphemous conclusion (and becomes a disbeliever) or he abandons his position (and his argument collapses).

Key Terms
الجبر
أهل الجنة
أبو الهذيل
الجهمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Abu Hudhayl's view is consistent with affirming Allah's power and does not lead to anthropomorphism.
Correction: The passage shows that if Allah directly creates every action (including eating, drinking, etc.), then He becomes the subject of those actions, which is clearly anthropomorphism and blasphemy.

Checkpoint

A) Either accept that Allah does evil or deny His power
B) Either accept that Allah performs all human actions or abandon his doctrine
C) Either accept that humans have free will or deny divine justice
D) Either accept that Paradise is eternal or that it is temporal

Key Takeaway
Abu Hudhayl's view that Paradise dwellers are compelled leads to the blasphemous conclusion that Allah performs their actions, forcing an unacceptable dilemma.

The Mu'tazilite Reliance on Dualist Principles
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite adoption of Dualist principles and their analogical reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi exposes the Mu'tazilite borrowing from the Dualists and refutes their analogy from the witnessed world.

Core Idea
Mu'tazilites borrowed core principles from Dualists but contradicted themselves on evil entities.

This passage from Nasafi's Tabṣirat al-Adilla uncovers a historical dependency: the Mu'tazilites adopted two key principles from Dualist sects—that creating evil is evil and that an act devoid of benefit is foolishness. However, Nasafi shows how they diverged on the application, particularly regarding evil entities, and how their reliance on analogy from the witnessed world ultimately refutes them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the historical claim and argument flow.
Notice the mention of Dualists and the two principles.
2
Identify the two shared principles
Find where the Mu'tazilites agree with the Dualists: creating evil is evil, and an act devoid of benefit is foolishness.
3
Analyze the point of divergence
See how the Mu'tazilites disagree with Dualists on evil entities and benefit, leading to internal inconsistency.
Consider the role of analogy from the witnessed world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما تلقنوا هذا من إخوانهم الثنوية فبنوا المذهب على قواعدهم ونسجوا على منوالهم، فزعموا أن إيجاد القبيح قبيح وأن ما خلا عن النفع فهو سفه وتعلقوا بالشاهد، غير أن الثنوية يزعمون أن ما لا منفعة فيه للفاعل فهو قبيح، ولهذا زعموا أن كل فعل للنور فهو في نفع نفسه هو وخلاصه عن وثائق الظلمة، فصوبت المعتزلة الثنوية في هاتين القاعدتين وزعمت أن إيجاد القبيح قبيح وهو سفه، غير أنهم خالفوا الثنوية في تخليق الأجسام المستخبثة المستقذرة والأعيان الضارة، فزعمت الثنوية أنها لما كانت قبيحة كان إيجادها قبيحا وهي محدثة فلابد لها من صانع آخر، وأنكرت المعتزلة قبحها مع شهادة الله تعالى بخبثها وتشبيه الكفر الخبيث بها بقوله: ﴿ وَمَثَلُ كَلِمَةٍ خَبِيثَةٍ ﴾ (سورة إبراهيم - 14 - الآية 26) وزعمت أن القردة والخنازير وإبليس - عليهم اللعنة - ومردة الشياطين كلهم أشياء مستحسنة لا خبث فيها ولا قبح، وإنما القبح في الأفعال، فأنكروا أن يكون الله تعالى خالقًا لها، ولهذا أنكروا حرمة الأعيان النازلة من حرمة الأفعال منزلة الحفظ من الحماية لما لا حرمة إلا بوصف القبح ولا قبح في الأعيان لما أنها لو كانت قبيحة لم يكن إيجاها حكمة، وكذا خالفوا الثنوية في تعليق الحكمة بنفع الفاعل نفسه بل علقوها بمطلق النفع إما للفاعل وإما لغيره، وتمسك الفريقان - أعني المعتزلة والثنوية - في الأمرين جميعًا أعني - أن إيجاد القبيح قبيح وأن الفعل الخالي عن النفع سفه – بالشاهد، فنوقضت المعتزلة بما بينا أن الباري يرى عبده يزني بأمته ويخلى بينهما مع القدرة
And they learned this only from their brothers the Dualists, so they built the doctrine on their principles and wove on their pattern. They claimed that creating evil is evil and that what is devoid of benefit is foolishness, and they clung to the witnessed. However, the Dualists claim that what has no benefit for the agent is evil, and for that reason they claimed that every action of the light is for its own benefit and its liberation from the bonds of darkness. So the Mu'tazilites approved of the Dualists in these two principles and claimed that creating evil is evil and it is foolishness. But they disagreed with the Dualists regarding the creation of impure, filthy bodies and harmful entities. The Dualists claimed that since they are evil, their creation is evil, and they are originated, so they must have another creator. The Mu'tazilites denied their evil despite God's testimony of their impurity and His likening evil unbelief to them in His saying: {And the example of a bad word} (Surah Ibrahim 14:26), and they claimed that monkeys, pigs, Iblis—curses be upon them—and the rebellious devils are all commendable things with no impurity or evil in them, and that evil is only in actions. So they denied that God is their Creator, and for this they denied the prohibition of entities descending from the prohibition of actions in the station of preservation from protection, because there is no prohibition except by the description of evil, and there is no evil in entities, because if they were evil, their creation would not be wisdom. Similarly, they disagreed with the Dualists in attaching wisdom to the benefit of the agent himself, but rather attached it to absolute benefit, either for the agent or for another. And both groups—namely the Mu'tazilites and the Dualists—held fast in both matters, i.e., that creating evil is evil and that an act devoid of benefit is foolishness, to the witnessed. So the Mu'tazilites were refuted by what we have shown: that the Creator sees His slave committing adultery with his slave-girl and leaves them alone with [His] power...
Source pages p. 926

Commentary
This passage is a historical and doctrinal refutation of the Mu'tazilite position by exposing its origin. Nasafi states that the Mu'tazilites learned their principles from the Dualists (al-Thanawiyya, possibly Manichaeans or Zoroastrians). The Dualists held that creating evil is evil and that an act devoid of benefit for the agent is foolishness. The Mu'tazilites agreed on these two principles but diverged on whether certain entities (e.g., impure bodies) are evil. While Dualists argued that such entities are evil and must have a separate creator, the Mu'tazilites denied their evil, even using a Quranic verse to liken evil speech to evil entities, yet they claimed that animals and devils are commendable, confining evil to actions. They thus denied that God creates evil entities, which leads to denying divine prohibition of such entities. They also disagreed with Dualists on the criterion of benefit: Dualists said benefit must be for the agent himself, Mu'tazilites said benefit could be for the agent or another. Both groups, however, based their arguments on analogy from the witnessed world. Nasafi then points out that the Mu'tazilites are refuted by the earlier example: God allows a slave to commit adultery despite having power to prevent it, which is not considered evil in the unseen, thus breaking their analogy.

Key Terms
الثنوية
المعتزلة
إيجاد القبيح
النفع
الشاهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilite view is purely rational and original.
Correction: The passage shows their principles are historically dependent on Dualist premises, not purely rational.

Checkpoint
What two principles did the Mu'tazilites adopt from the Dualists according to Nasafi?
A) That God creates all actions and that benefit is only for the agent.
B) That creating evil is evil and that an act devoid of benefit is foolishness.
C) That evil entities have a separate creator and that benefit must be for the agent.
D) That good and evil are known by reason alone and that God must act for a purpose.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites' principles are not original but borrowed, and their reliance on analogy from the witnessed world fails when applied to the unseen.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever uncritically accepted a belief or principle because it seemed reasonable, only to later realize its source was flawed? How can you guard against borrowing ideas that contradict revelation?
?
When you encounter a creature or entity you consider harmful or evil, do you attribute its existence to God's wisdom or to some other cause? How does this passage challenge your default assumption?
?
If your reasoning about God leads you to conclusions that contradict clear Quranic statements—like the impurity of certain beings—are you willing to abandon your reasoning? What is at stake if you don't?

5 min
Mu'tazila adopt Dualist principles on evil and benefit
Nasafi recounts Mu'tazila borrowing Dualist principles on evil and benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila adopted two key principles from the Dualists and where they diverged.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila borrowed Dualist ideas but reshaped them to fit their own theology.

In this passage, al-Nasafi explains that the Mu'tazila derived their positions on evil and benefit from the Dualist (Majus) tradition. They agreed that creating evil is itself evil and that an action without any benefit is foolish, but they modified the scope of benefit and denied that physical objects can be intrinsically evil. This sets the stage for subsequent refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument unfold before you interpret.
Notice how Nasafi first states the borrowing, then the two principles, then the differences.
2
Identify the two principles
The passage states two principles the Mu'tazila accepted from the Dualists: that bringing about evil is evil, and that an action devoid of benefit is foolishness (safah).
3
Spot the differences
The Mu'tazila disagreed on two points: they denied that created entities (like pigs, Iblis) are inherently evil, and they broadened benefit to include benefit for others, not just the agent.
This shows they did not accept Dualist teachings wholesale.
4
Note the method of argument
Both groups relied on analogy with the witnessed world (al-shahid) to support their claims. This is a key methodological point.
Understanding this helps see why later refutations focus on the limits of analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما تلقنوا هذا من إخوانهم الثنوية فبنوا المذهب على قواعدهم ونسجوا على منوالهم، فزعموا أن إيجاد القبيح قبيح وأن ما خلا عن النفع فهو سفه وتعلقوا بالشاهد، غير أن الثنوية يزعمون أن ما لا منفعة فيه للفاعل فهو قبيح، ولهذا زعموا أن كل فعل للنور فهو في نفع نفسه هو وخلاصه عن وثائق الظلمة، فصوبت المعتزلة الثنوية في هاتين القاعدتين وزعمت أن إيجاد القبيح قبيح وهو سفه، غير أنهم خالفوا الثنوية في تخليق الأجسام المستخبثة المستقذرة والأعيان الضارة، فزعمت الثنوية أنها لما كانت قبيحة كان إيجادها قبيحا وهي محدثة فلابد لها من صانع آخر، وأنكرت المعتزلة قبحها مع شهادة الله تعالى بخبثها وتشبيه الكفر الخبيث بها بقوله: ﴿ وَمَثَلُ كَلِمَةٍ خَبِيثَةٍ ﴾ وزعمت أن القردة والخنازير وإبليس عليهم اللعنة ومردة الشياطين كلهم أشياء مستحسنة لا خبث فيها ولا قبح، وإنما القبح في الأفعال، فأنكروا أن يكون الله تعالى خالقًا لها، ولهذا أنكروا حرمة الأعيان النازلة من حرمة الأفعال منزلة الحفظ من الحماية لما لا حرمة إلا بوصف القبح ولا قبح في الأعيان لما أنها لو كانت قبيحة لم يكن إيجاها حكمة، وكذا خالفوا الثنوية في تعليق الحكمة بنفع الفاعل نفسه بل علقوها بمطلق النفع إما للفاعل وإما لغيره، وتمسك الفريقان أعني المعتزلة والثنوية في الأمرين جميعًا أعني أن إيجاد القبيح قبيح وأن الفعل الخالي عن النفع سفه بالشاهد.
And they only learned this from their brothers the Dualists, so they built the school on their principles and wove on their loom, and claimed that bringing about evil is evil and that what lacks benefit is foolishness, and they clung to the witnessed world. But the Dualists claim that what has no benefit for the agent is evil, and for this they claimed that every act of light is for its own benefit and its deliverance from the bonds of darkness. So the Mu'tazila approved the Dualists in these two principles and claimed that bringing about evil is evil and it is foolishness, but they differed from the Dualists regarding the creation of loathsome, filthy bodies and harmful entities. The Dualists claimed that since they are evil, bringing them about is evil, and they are originated, so they must have another maker. The Mu'tazila denied their evil despite God's testimony of their foulness and His likening foul unbelief to them in His saying: 'And the example of a bad word' (Ibrahim 26). And they claimed that monkeys, pigs, Iblis—curses upon them—and the rebellious devils are all commendable things with no foulness or evil in them, and evil is only in actions. So they denied that God is their Creator, and for this they denied the prohibition of entities, transferring the prohibition from actions to the level of preservation from protection, because there is no prohibition except by the attribute of evil, and there is no evil in entities because if they were evil, bringing them about would not be wisdom. And likewise they differed from the Dualists in attaching wisdom to the benefit of the agent himself, but rather attached it to absolute benefit, either for the agent or for others. And both groups—meaning the Mu'tazila and the Dualists—clung to the witnessed world in both matters, namely that bringing about evil is evil and that action devoid of benefit is foolishness.
Source pages p. 926

Commentary
This passage describes how the Mu'tazila adopted two key principles from the Dualists (Thaniyya): that creating evil is itself evil, and that an action completely devoid of benefit is foolish (safah). The author first notes that the Dualists specify benefit as being for the agent himself, citing their example that every act of light (the good principle) is for its own benefit. The Mu'tazila agreed on the two principles but differed on two points: first, they denied that certain created entities (like pigs, monkeys, Iblis) are inherently evil, arguing instead that evil lies only in actions; second, they broadened the scope of benefit to include benefit for others, not just the agent. Both groups, however, based their arguments on analogy with the witnessed world (al-shahid), i.e., human experience. The passage sets up the Mu'tazili position that will be refuted subsequently.

Key Terms

القبيح
النفع
السفه

الحكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila and Dualists agreed on everything regarding evil and benefit.
Correction: They agreed on the two principles but differed on whether entities can be evil (Mu'tazila said no) and on the scope of benefit (Mu'tazila included benefit for others).

Checkpoint
What did the Mu'tazila accept from the Dualists?
A) That creating evil is evil and action devoid of benefit is foolish
B) That all created entities are evil
C) That benefit must be for the agent alone
D) That God creates evil actions

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila adopted the Dualist principles of evil and benefit but modified them to exclude inherent evil in objects and to include benefit for others.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the world, do you also judge God's actions based on your own sense of benefit? Have you ever wondered why there is evil in creation? What does this lesson tell you about trusting God's wisdom even when you don't see the benefit?
?
The Mu'tazila rejected that God creates evil entities because they thought it would be foolish. Do you ever find yourself questioning God's wisdom when you face hardship or see something you dislike? What would change in your life if you truly believed that everything God creates has a purpose, even if you cannot see it?
?
The Dualists thought light acts only for its own benefit. Do you ever act only for your own gain, even in worship? How does this lesson challenge you to seek benefit for others as well?

The Objection of Benefit and the Invalidation of the Mu'tazilite Analogy
Nasafi presents a dialectical objection against the Mu'tazila, using their own method of analogy to force a dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazilite reliance on analogy with the witnessed world leads to a theological dilemma regarding God's wisdom.

Core Idea
If God is like us, He must benefit Himself; but He does not.

Nasafi presents a powerful objection that turns the Mu'tazilites' own method against them. If they insist that all actions are evaluated by the standard of the witnessed world, then they must conclude that God is not wise—or that He is needy. This dilemma shows the limits of anthropomorphic analogy in theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Identify the claim that actions are only good if they benefit the agent, as the Dualists held.
The objection uses the Mu'tazilite's own principle of analogy (shahid).
2
See the dilemma
Notice how the objection forces the Mu'tazila into either denying God's wisdom or attributing need to Him.
3
Understand the invalidation
The Mu'tazila cannot escape without abandoning their own analogy. If they reject both options, they invalidate their method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل لهم: ما تنكرون على من يقول لا يحسن الفعل في الشاهد إلا لنفع نفسه كما زعمت الثنوية، ومن نفع غيره إنما حسن فعله لأن من نفع غيره نفع نفسه في الحقيقة إنما بنيل الثواب، وإما باكتساب الجاه، وإما بإحراز المحمدة والثناء الجميل حتى إن من نفع غيره بلا حمد يستحق ولا أجر ينال لا يكون ذلك حكمة، فإن تمسكتم بالشاهد فقولوا: إن الله تعالى ليس بحكيم لما أنه لا ينفع بفعله، وإن قلتم: إنه حكيم فقولوا: إنه منتفع محل للحاجات دافع للضرورات، فبأي أمر تمسكوا خرجوا عن الدين، وإن أبوهما أبطلوا استشهادهم بالشاهد، وإذا بطل ذلك فبعد ذلك جمهور متكلمي أهل الحديث يقولون: القبيح ما نهى عنه، والله تعالى ليس بمنهي عن إيجاد الكفر، والعبد منهي عن اكتساب الكفر فكان خلقه تعالى غير قبيح، وكسب العبد قبيحا على هذا يتكلم جمهور متكلمي أهل الحديث، وبعضهم يقولون: القبيح ما يعود به على فاعله الضرر المحض، والله تعالى لا يعود عليه بفعل ما ضرر، فلا يكون خلقه الكفر قبيحًا منه لعدم عود ضرر به عليه، والكافر يعود باكتسابه الكفر عليه ضرر محض فكان اكتسابه الكفر قبيحا، ويجعل الذم والحمد من توابع هذين الفعلين فنقول: إن الفعل الذي يستحق عليه الحمد ما لا يعود به ضرر على فاعله، والذي
And it was said to them: What do you deny of one who says that an action in the witnessed world is only good for the benefit of oneself, as the Dualists claimed, and that whoever benefits others, his action is only good because whoever benefits others benefits himself in reality either by obtaining reward, or by acquiring prestige, or by attaining praise and beautiful commendation, such that if one benefits others without deserving praise or receiving any reward, that is not wisdom. So if you cling to the witnessed world, say that God is not wise because He does not benefit by His action. And if you say that He is wise, then say that He is benefited, subject to needs, repeller of necessities. Whichever thing they cling to, they leave the religion. And if they refuse both, they invalidate their own citation of the witnessed world. And when that is invalidated, then after that the majority of theologians of the People of Hadith say: Evil is that which is forbidden. God is not forbidden to bring about unbelief, while the servant is forbidden to acquire unbelief, so His creation (of unbelief) is not evil, and the servant's acquisition (of unbelief) is evil. This is how the majority of theologians of the People of Hadith speak. And some of them say: Evil is that which returns upon its agent pure harm. God does not suffer any harm from an action, so His creation of unbelief is not evil from Him due to the absence of harm returning upon Him. The disbeliever, by acquiring unbelief, returns upon himself pure harm, so his acquisition of unbelief is evil. And they make blame and praise follow from these two actions. So we say: The action for which one deserves praise is that which does not return harm upon its agent, and which...
Source pages p. 927

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical objection against the Mu'tazila and then states the Sunni position. The objection is: If you insist on analogy with the witnessed world, then either you say benefit is only for the agent (as Dualists hold) or you open the door to including rewards, prestige, etc. Either way, God neither benefits Himself nor receives reward, so He would not be wise. If you reject both, you abandon your own analogy. After invalidating the Mu'tazila position, the author presents the Sunni definition: The majority (jumhur) of theologians of Ahl al-Hadith define evil (qabih) as 'what is forbidden' (ma nuha 'anhu). Since God is not forbidden to create unbelief, His creation is not evil; the servant's acquisition is evil because he is forbidden. An alternative definition among them is that evil is 'what returns pure harm upon its agent'; God suffers no harm, so His act is not evil. Thus blame and praise attach accordingly. The text cuts off at 'and which', likely continuing the explanation.

الثنوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the Mu'tazilite use of analogy is logically sound and can be consistently applied to God.
Correction: This passage shows that the analogy leads to an absurd dilemma: either God is not wise or He is needy. Thus, analogy with the witnessed world is insufficient for understanding divine attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, what happens if the Mu'tazila insist on analogy with the witnessed world?
A) They must affirm that God is wise.
B) They must conclude that God is not wise or that He is needy.
C) They can consistently maintain their position.
D) They must reject the concept of wisdom altogether.

Key Takeaway
Analogizing God to the witnessed world inevitably leads to contradictions; the Mu'tazilite method is self-defeating.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever thought you understood God by comparing Him to yourself? What dangerous assumptions might that lead to, and how does this lesson expose that? Commit to one area where you will stop using human analogies for God.
?
If you catch yourself thinking 'God wouldn't do that because I wouldn't', what does this passage demand you do about that thought? Write down a specific habit of comparison you must abandon starting today.
?
This argument shows that analogizing God leads to contradictions. What specific comparison do you need to abandon today to protect your tawhid? Be honest—name it and decide how you will replace that thought with awe of God's transcendence.
