The Mu'tazila Doctrine of al-Aslah
Al-Nasafi presents the Mu'tazila argument that Allah must do the utmost good.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument that Allah is obligated to do the utmost good for every creature, including disbelievers.

Core Idea
Allah must do the maximum possible good for all, even for Abu Jahl.

The Mu'tazila built a rigorous argument about divine justice, claiming that Allah is obliged to do what is most beneficial for His creatures. In this passage, al-Nasafi summarizes their reasoning, including a striking example that equates the Prophet Muhammad with Abu Jahl. Let's examine their logic closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the full argument. Notice the conditional structure: if Allah had power to do a lutf and didn't, He would be unjust.
The argument hinges on the concept of 'lutf' (grace that would cause belief).
2
Identify the main claims
The Mu'tazila deny that Allah has a lutf that would make disbelievers believe, because if He had it and withheld it, He would be unjust. Then they assert that Allah must do the utmost good for every servant.
They equate the benefit given to the Prophet and Abu Jahl.
3
Examine the example of Prophet and Abu Jahl
They say Allah does the same level of good for Abu Jahl as for the Prophet. This illustrates that they deny any special favor that others don't receive.
This example is controversial and central to the debate.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this argument is problematic from a Sunni perspective. What does it imply about Allah's freedom and mercy?
This connects to the broader issue of divine obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم جمهور المعتزلة أن ليس في مقدور الله تعالى لطف لو فعل بالكفار لأمنوا، ولو كان ذلك في مقدوره ولم يفعل ولم يعطهم ذلك لكان سفيها بخيلاً جائراً ظالمًا مانعا حقا مستحقاً، وغاية ما يقدر الله تعالى عليه مما به صلاح الخلق واجب عليه، وفعل بكل عبد مؤمن أو كافر غاية ما هو في مقدوره من مصلحته، وكما فعل بالنبي عليه السلام ما هو في مقدوره من المصلحة فعل بأبي جهل مثله وليس له على النبي محمد عليه السلام إنعام ليس ذلك على أبي جهل، ولو كان ذلك لكان ظالمًا فيما فعل جائرًا مجابيًا بل فعل غاية ما في مقدوره من مصلحة أبي جهل وليس له أن يفعل بأحد ما هو المفسدة له البتة، هذا هو قول جمهورهم.
And the majority of the Mu'tazila claimed that there is not in the power of Allah the Exalted a lutf that if He did to the disbelievers they would believe; and if that were in His power and He did not do it and did not give it to them, He would be foolish, miserly, unjust, oppressive, preventing a due right that is deserved. And the utmost that Allah is capable of that brings about good for creation is obligatory upon Him; and He does for every servant, believer or disbeliever, the utmost of what is in His power that brings about his benefit. And just as He did for the Prophet, peace be upon him, what is in His power of benefit, He did for Abu Jahl the like of it; and He does not have upon the Prophet Muhammad, peace be upon him, a favor that He does not have upon Abu Jahl. And if that were the case, He would be an oppressor in what He did, unjust, oppressive; rather, He did the utmost of what is in His power of benefit for Abu Jahl, and He cannot do to anyone that which is detrimental to him at all. This is the statement of their majority.
Source pages p. 994

Commentary
This passage presents the argument of the majority of the Mu'tazila on the issue of al-Aslah. They deny that Allah has in His power a grace (lutf) that would cause disbelievers to believe, because if He had such power and did not use it, He would be acting foolishly, miserly, and unjustly. They assert that Allah must do the utmost good possible for every creature. They give an example: Allah does the same level of benefit for the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) as for Abu Jahl, meaning there is no special favor for the Prophet that Abu Jahl does not receive. If there were, Allah would be unjust. Therefore, Allah does the maximum possible good for everyone and never does anything detrimental. This is the doctrine of their majority.

Key Terms
الأصلح
اللطف
المصلحة
المفسدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What reason do the Mu'tazila give for denying that Allah has a lutf that would make disbelievers believe?
A) Because it would contradict human free will.
B) Because if He had it and did not use it, He would be unjust and miserly.
C) Because such a lutf is impossible for Allah to create.
D) Because it would make belief compulsory, removing test.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that Allah is obligated to do the utmost good for every creature, and any special favor would be unjust.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever thought that Allah should treat you better than someone else, like a special favor? How does the Mu'tazila claim that even the Prophet has no favor over Abu Jahl expose the arrogance in that expectation? Will you repent from that ingratitude today?
?
If you truly believed Allah owes you the best possible life, how would your worship change? Would it become a transaction rather than humble surrender? Look at your own heart: do you serve Allah expecting certain outcomes, or out of love regardless of what you receive?

Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir's Doctrine of Lutf and Maslaha
Al-Nasafi explains Bishr's distinction between obligatory benefit and non-obligatory optimum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Bishr's distinction between obligatory benefit (maslaha) and non-obligatory optimum (al-aslah), and the concept of lutf (grace) that Allah may give but is not obligated to provide.

Core Idea
Allah must do good, but not necessarily the best—lest His power be limited.

Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir, the head of the Baghdad Mu'tazila, introduced a nuanced view on divine obligations. He argued that while Allah must act for His servants' benefit, He is not required to do the absolute best. This opens the door for the concept of lutf—a special grace that could lead to belief but is not compulsory. This passage lays out that distinction and its theological implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the argument.
Notice the key terms: المصلحة, الأصلح, اللطف.
2
Identify the two categories
Find where Bishr distinguishes between what is obligatory (maslaha) and what is not (al-aslah).
Also note the term 'Ashab al-Lutf' at the end.
3
Consider the logical argument
Bishr argues that obligating the most beneficial would imply a limit to Allah's power. Why would that be? Reflect on the idea of a finite maximum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال بشر بن المعتمر رئيس معتزلة بغداد ومن تابعه من أصحابه: إن الله تعالى لا يجوز أن يفعل بعبده ما هو المفسدة له بل يجب عليه أن يفعل به ما هو المصلحة له لكن لا يجب عليه أن يفعل ما هو الأصلح إذ ليس لما في مقدور الله تعالى من المصلحة واللطف غاية لما في القول بإيجاب الأصلح القول بتناهي مقدور الله تعالى: وذلك محال بل هو تعالى قادر على لطف لو فعل لأمنوا اختيارا وأحدث بهم إيمانًا يستحقون على الله تعالى الثواب مثل ما يستحقونه لو آمنوا مع عدمه، ولا يجب عليه إعطاء ذلك اللطف، وإنما يجب عليه إعطاء ما هو صلاح لهم وإزاحة عللهم فيما يحتاجون إليه لأداء ما كلفهم وما تيسر عليهم مع وجوده العمل بما أمرهم به، وقد فعل ذلك بهم. هذا هو المشهور من مذهبه ومذهب أتباعه، وعامة المعتزلة يسمونهم أصحاب اللطف.
And Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir, the head of the Mu'tazila of Baghdad, and those of his associates who followed him, said: Indeed, Allah the Exalted may not do to His servant that which is harmful; rather, He must do to him that which is beneficial. But He is not obliged to do that which is most beneficial, because there is no limit to what is in Allah's power of benefit and grace. For the assertion of obligating the most beneficial leads to asserting the finitude of Allah's power, and that is impossible. Rather, He, exalted, is capable of a grace that if He did, they would believe voluntarily and He would create in them a faith, by which they deserve reward from Allah the Exalted like what they would deserve if they believed without it; and He is not obliged to give that grace. Rather, He is only obliged to give what is beneficial for them and to remove their excuses regarding what they need for performing what He has charged them, and to make easy for them, with its existence, acting upon what He commanded them; and He has done that with them. This is the well-known (view) of his school and the school of his followers, and the generality of the Mu'tazila call them 'Ashab al-Lutf' (People of Grace).
Source pages p. 995

Commentary
This passage presents the view of Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir, the leader of the Baghdad Mu'tazila, and his followers. They make a distinction between doing what is beneficial (maslaha) and doing what is most beneficial (al-aslah). They argue that Allah cannot do harm to His servant; He must do what is beneficial. However, He is not obligated to do the most beneficial, because that would imply a limit to His power (since the most beneficial would be a finite maximum), which is impossible. Allah is capable of a grace (lutf) that would cause disbelievers to believe voluntarily and earn reward, but He is not obliged to give it. What He is obliged to do is to give what is beneficial and to remove obstacles for the performance of duties. This view is known among the Mu'tazila, who call this group 'Ashab al-Lutf' (People of Grace).

Key Terms
اللطف
المصلحة
الأصلح
أصحاب اللطف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think all Mu'tazila believe Allah must do the most beneficial (al-aslah).
Correction: Bishr and his followers distinguish between maslaha (obligatory) and al-aslah (not obligatory), allowing for lutf.

Checkpoint
How does Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir distinguish between maslaha and al-aslah?
A) He says both are obligatory.
B) He says only maslaha is obligatory, not al-aslah.
C) He says only al-aslah is obligatory.
D) He says neither is obligatory.

Key Takeaway
Bishr taught that Allah must benefit but not necessarily optimize, and that lutf is a possible but non-obligatory grace.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Bishr teaches that Allah is not obligated to give you the most beneficial situation, only what is beneficial. Does your heart secretly resent that you don't have the 'optimal' life you imagine? Will you today thank Allah for what He gave, trusting that His withholding is wisdom, not stinginess?
?
This lesson says Allah is capable of a grace that would force you to believe, but He does not give it. Your faith is not compelled; it is a gift He chose to give without obligation. Does that humble you? How will you respond to such a gift with gratitude and action?
?
If Allah is only obliged to remove excuses for what He commanded, then every difficulty you face that does not prevent obedience is not an excuse. Are you using hardships as excuses to disobey? What would it look like to obey even when it's hard, knowing you have no excuse?

Ja'far ibn Harb's View on Lutf and His Retraction
Reports Ja'far ibn Harb's view on lutf and al-aslah and his retraction due to logical inconsistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical debate around Ja'far ibn Harb's view that God withholds a grace (lutf) that would cause voluntary faith, and why he retracted due to logical inconsistency.

Core Idea
Ja'far ibn Harb initially held that withholding lutf is aslah, but retracted because it contradicts free will.

This passage from the Tabṣirat al-Adilla reports a specific view held by the early Mu'tazilite theologian Ja'far ibn Harb regarding divine grace (lutf) and what is most beneficial (al-aslah). It also narrates his eventual retraction of this view, highlighting a key logical argument from his contemporaries. Understanding this debate is essential for grasping the internal diversity and reasoning within early Mu'tazilite kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the narrative of Ja'far's view and retraction come through.
Notice the logical argument against Ja'far's position.
2
Identify Ja'far's original claim
Ja'far said there is a lutf that would cause voluntary belief, but withholding it is aslah for greater reward.
3
Understand the retraction and its reason
Al-Ka'bi reports Ja'far retracted because his view combined inevitability (darurah) with choice (ikhtiyar), which is contradictory.
This is a key logical concept in kalam.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why this retraction matters for Mu'tazilite theology. It shows the importance of logical consistency.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بشر قال : وذكر - يعني أبا الحسين - أنه بلغه عن أبي موسى المردار أنه كان يحكي التوبة عنه وكان جعفر بن حرب يقول : إن عند الله تعالى لطفا لو أعطاه الكافرين لأمنوا اختيارًا إيماناً لا يستحقون عليه من الثواب ما يستحقون به إذا آمنوا مع عدم ذلك اللطف، والأصلح لهم ما فعل بهم من تركه إعطائهم ذلك اللطف؛ لأن (١) الله تعالى لا يعرض عباده إلا لأعلى المنازل وأشرفها وأفضل الثواب وأكثرها قال الكعبي : ثم ترك جعفر بن حرب هذا القول ورجع إلى قول أصحابه من أن ذلك محال؛ لأنه إذا كان الإيمان يقع منهم عند حدوث اللطف لا محالة فهو واقع ضرورة ولو لم يكن (۲) ضرورة جاز أن لا يقع ولا يوجد، إذا قال قائل: هو واقع لا يكن(۲) محالة ثم قال: هو اختيار فقد ناقض وجمع بين الاختيار والضرورة وذلك محال، قال الكعبي: كتب إلي توبة جعفر من هذا القول أبو الحسين، والأمر في ذلك مشهور. ثم الأصلح عند البغداديين منهم ما هو الأصلح في (۳) الحكمة والتدبير، وعند بعض البصريين منهم الصلاح هو النفع والأصلح هو الأنفع(٤).
وقد اتفق البصريون والبغداديون من المعتزلة في القول بأنه يجب الثواب على مشاق التكليف والأعواض عن الآلام غير المستحقة، وأجمعوا على أن الله تعالى إذا خلق عبدًا وأكمل عقله فلا يتركه هملا، بل يجب عليه أن يفكره ويمكنه من نيل المراشد، فإذا كلف عبدًا وجب في حكمته أن يلطف به ويفعل أقصى ممكن في معلومه مما يؤمن ويطيع المكلف عنده. ومما اتفقوا على وجوبه إحباط الطاعات بالفسوق وقبول التوبة.
Bishr said: He mentioned—meaning Abu al-Husayn—that he had heard from Abu Musa al-Murdar that he used to relate the repentance from him, and Ja'far ibn Harb used to say: Indeed, with Allah the Exalted is a grace (lutf) such that if He gave it to the disbelievers, they would voluntarily believe, a faith for which they would not deserve the reward that they would deserve if they believed without that grace (lutf), and the most beneficial (al-aslah) for them is what He did with them by refraining from giving them that grace (lutf), because (1) Allah the Exalted does not present to His servants except the highest stations and the most honorable and the best reward and the greatest. Al-Ka'bi said: Then Ja'far ibn Harb abandoned this statement and returned to the statement of his companions that that is impossible (muhal); because if faith occurs from them when grace (lutf) occurs inevitably, then it is real by necessity (darurah), and if it were not (2) necessity, it would be permissible that it does not occur and does not exist. If someone says: It occurs inevitably (la mahalah), then says: It is voluntary (ikhtiyar), then he has contradicted and combined between choice and necessity, and that is impossible (muhal). Al-Ka'bi said: Abu al-Husayn wrote to me about Ja'far's repentance from this statement, and the matter regarding that is well-known. Then al-aslah according to the Baghdadis among them is that which is optimal in (3) wisdom (hikmah) and administration (tadbir), and according to some of the Basrans among them, al-salah is benefit (naf') and al-aslah is the more beneficial (anfa') (4).
And the Basrans and Baghdadis of the Mu'tazilites agreed in the statement that reward must be for the hardships of obligation and compensations for undeserved pains, and they unanimously agreed that if Allah creates a servant and perfects his intellect, He does not leave him neglected, rather He must make him think and enable him to attain guidance. So if He obligates a servant, it is necessary in His wisdom to grant him grace (lutf) and do the utmost possible in His knowledge of what causes the obligated to believe and obey. And among what they agreed upon as obligatory is the nullification of acts of obedience by transgression and the acceptance of repentance.
Source pages p. 996

Commentary
This passage reports a historical debate among early Mu'tazilites—specifically Ja'far ibn Harb and his retraction—and then distinguishes two views on the meaning of al-aslah among the Baghdadis and Basrans. The passage begins by narrating that Abu al-Husayn (al-Basri) heard from Abu Musa al-Murdar that Ja'far ibn Harb held a particular view: that there is a divine grace (lutf) such that if God gave it to disbelievers, they would voluntarily believe, but without that grace they deserve more reward. Ja'far considered that the most beneficial (al-aslah) for them is that God withholds that grace, because He only presents the highest stations. Then al-Ka'bi reports that Ja'far abandoned this view and returned to his companions' position that it is impossible (muhal). The reason is that if faith necessarily occurs when grace occurs, then it is by necessity. If one says it occurs inevitably and then says it is voluntary, that is a contradiction between choice and necessity. Then the passage notes the repentance of Ja'far. Finally, it gives the different definitions of al-aslah: the Baghdadis define it as what is optimal in wisdom and administration, while some Basrans define salah as benefit and al-aslah as more beneficial. The footnote adds that Mu'tazilites agreed on the obligation of reward for hardships and that God must grant grace to the obligated.

Key Terms
اللطف (lutf)
الأصلح (al-aslah)
الاختيار (ikhtiyar)
الضرورة (darurah)
المحال (muhal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ja'far ibn Harb's view that God withholds lutf for greater reward is a standard Mu'tazilite position.
Correction: Ja'far's view was considered logically contradictory and he retracted it; mainstream Mu'tazilites held that such a combination of inevitability and choice is impossible.

Checkpoint
What was the main reason for Ja'far ibn Harb's retraction according to al-Ka'bi?
A) He was forced by Abu al-Husayn.
B) His view contradicted the Ash'ari position.
C) His view combined necessity and choice, which is impossible.
D) He realized God does not have lutf at all.

Key Takeaway
Ja'far ibn Harb retracted his view on lutf because it impossibly combined necessity and free will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief that you later realized was logically inconsistent? What did it cost you to admit the error, and did you retract it publicly like Ja'far?
?
This passage shows a theologian retracting a view because it contradicted free will. In your own life, are there areas where you try to hold onto contradictory ideas about God's justice and human responsibility rather than accepting the logical tension?
?
Ja'far's retraction was famous because he was willing to abandon a previously held position for the sake of truth. Ask yourself: Is there a cherished belief you cling to that cannot withstand honest logical scrutiny? Will you have the courage to write your own 'tawbah' from it today?

Mu'tazilite Definitions of al-Aslah and Agreements on Divine Obligations
Defines al-aslah according to Baghdadis and Basrans and lists Mu'tazilite agreements on divine obligations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main definitions of al-aslah among Mu'tazilites and their agreements on divine obligations regarding lutf, reward, and punishment.

Core Idea
Mu'tazilites agreed that God must do the aslah for His servants, but defined it differently as wise administration or greater benefit.

The previous lesson focused on a controversial individual view; here we turn to the broader Mu'tazilite consensus. This passage reports that while the Baghdadis and Basrans differed on the precise meaning of al-aslah, they agreed on several key doctrines: that God must provide reward for hardship, that He must guide the intellect, and that He must grant lutf to the obligated. Understanding these agreements is crucial for grasping the Mu'tazilite system of divine justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the two definitions of al-aslah and then the agreements.
2
Identify the two definitions of al-aslah
The Baghdadis define it as what is optimal in wisdom and administration. Some Basrans define it as the more beneficial.
This shows diversity within Mu'tazilism.
3
Notice the agreements
All Mu'tazilites agree on reward for hardships, guidance of intellect, obligation of lutf, and nullification of acts by sin.
These are core Mu'tazilite teachings.
4
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself how the difference in defining al-aslah affects their understanding of divine justice.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بشر قال : وذكر - يعني أبا الحسين - أنه بلغه عن أبي موسى المردار أنه كان يحكي التوبة عنه وكان جعفر بن حرب يقول : إن عند الله تعالى لطفا لو أعطاه الكافرين لأمنوا اختيارًا إيماناً لا يستحقون عليه من الثواب ما يستحقون به إذا آمنوا مع عدم ذلك اللطف، والأصلح لهم ما فعل بهم من تركه إعطائهم ذلك اللطف؛ لأن (١) الله تعالى لا يعرض عباده إلا لأعلى المنازل وأشرفها وأفضل الثواب وأكثرها قال الكعبي : ثم ترك جعفر بن حرب هذا القول ورجع إلى قول أصحابه من أن ذلك محال؛ لأنه إذا كان الإيمان يقع منهم عند حدوث اللطف لا محالة فهو واقع ضرورة ولو لم يكن (۲) ضرورة جاز أن لا يقع ولا يوجد، إذا قال قائل: هو واقع لا يكن(۲) محالة ثم قال: هو اختيار فقد ناقض وجمع بين الاختيار والضرورة وذلك محال، قال الكعبي: كتب إلي توبة جعفر من هذا القول أبو الحسين، والأمر في ذلك مشهور. ثم الأصلح عند البغداديين منهم ما هو الأصلح في (۳) الحكمة والتدبير، وعند بعض البصريين منهم الصلاح هو النفع والأصلح هو الأنفع(٤).
وقد اتفق البصريون والبغداديون من المعتزلة في القول بأنه يجب الثواب على مشاق التكليف والأعواض عن الآلام غير المستحقة، وأجمعوا على أن الله تعالى إذا خلق عبدًا وأكمل عقله فلا يتركه هملا، بل يجب عليه أن يفكره ويمكنه من نيل المراشد، فإذا كلف عبدًا وجب في حكمته أن يلطف به ويفعل أقصى ممكن في معلومه مما يؤمن ويطيع المكلف عنده. ومما اتفقوا على وجوبه إحباط الطاعات بالفسوق وقبول التوبة.
Bishr said: He mentioned—meaning Abu al-Husayn—that he had heard from Abu Musa al-Murdar that he used to relate the repentance from him, and Ja'far ibn Harb used to say: Indeed, with Allah the Exalted is a grace (lutf) such that if He gave it to the disbelievers, they would voluntarily believe, a faith for which they would not deserve the reward that they would deserve if they believed without that grace (lutf), and the most beneficial (al-aslah) for them is what He did with them by refraining from giving them that grace (lutf), because (1) Allah the Exalted does not present to His servants except the highest stations and the most honorable and the best reward and the greatest. According to the passage, how do the Baghdadis define al-aslah?
A) The most beneficial.
B) Optimal in wisdom and administration.

Mu'tazilites agreed on God's obligation to do the aslah, but differed on its definition: Baghdadis as wisdom, Basrans as greater benefit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to define what is 'best' for your own life, would you measure it by wisdom or by benefit? Which definition leads you to trust God more when His plan is difficult to understand?
?
The Mu'tazilites believed God must guide every rational being. Do you feel that God has guided you in a way that makes faith easy? If not, what does that say about your understanding of His justice and your own responsibility?
?
They taught that major sins can nullify previous acts of obedience. Is this a fearful or hopeful teaching for you? How does it affect your relationship with your past sins and your motivation to repent?

The Mu'tazilite Proof for al-Aslah (Divine Grace)
Mu'tazilite argument for al-aslah using analogy from human wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite rational argument that God must provide the most beneficial (al-aslah) for His servants, and the analogical reasoning they employ.

Core Idea
Human wisdom demands providing means; God's wisdom demands providing the most beneficial.

The Mu'tazilites argued that God's wisdom obligates Him to do what is most beneficial for His servants. They supported this with a powerful analogy drawn from human conduct. In this passage, we examine their foundational reasoning and the Quranic evidence they cited.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how the argument is constructed from a general principle about wise conduct.
The passage uses two analogies: a commander and a host.
2
Understand the analogy
Identify the key points of comparison: a wise person must provide means for obedience or reconciliation; God is wise, so He must do likewise.
The analogy is from human experience to divine action.
3
Apply the argument to God
See how the Mu'tazilites apply the same reasoning to God, citing His attributes and Quranic verses (7:168, 7:94) to support their conclusion.
They conclude that God always does the most beneficial for His servants in religion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شبهة المعتزلة في قولهم الأصلح]: وشبهتهم (٥) التي يعتمدون عليها أنا وجدنا الحكيم إذا كان أمرًا بطاعة محبًا لها مريدًا، فلن يجوز أن يمنع المأمور ما يصل به إلى طاعته إذا كان قادرا على أن يعطيه ذلك، وكان بذله إياه لا يخرجه من استحقاق الوصف بالحكمة ومنعه لا ينفعه(١)، وكذلك إذا كان له عدو يدعوه إلى موالاته ويجب رجوعه إلى طاعته فلن يجوز أن يعامله من الغلظ واللين إلا بما يعلم أنه أنجع فيما يريد منه، وأدعى له إلى ترك ما هو فيه من عداوته، فإن عرض له أمران من الشدة والغلظة والملاينة والملاطفة يعلم أن أحدهما أدعى لعدوه إلى المراجعة والإنابة، والآخر دون ذلك ففعل الأدون وترك أن يفعل الأصلح الأدعى وكلاهما في قدرته عليهما بمنزلة لا يضره بذلهما ولا ينفعه منعهما، كان عند الحكماء جميعًا مذموماً خارجاً عن استحقاق الوصف بالجود والحكمة، فلما كان هذا فيما بينا على ما وصفنا، وكان الله عز وجل قادرا رحيما جوادًا عالمًا بمواضع حاجة عبادة آمرا لهم بطاعته وترك عداوته والرجوع إلى ولايته لا يضره الإعطاء ولا ينفعه المنع ولا يلته منه ذم، علمنا أنه لا يفعل بهم إلا أصلح الأشياء لهم في دينهم وأدعى لهم إلى طاعته سقما كان ذلك أو صحة لذة أو ألمًا ، آمنوا أو كفروا ، أطاعوا أو عصوا قال الله تعالى: وَبَلَوْنَهُم بِالحَسَنَاتِ وَالسَّيِّئَاتِ لَعَلَّهُمْ يَرْجِعُونَ (٢) وقال تعالى: ﴿ وَمَا أَرْسَلْنَا فِي قَرْيَةٍ مِّن نَّبِيِّ إِلَّا أَخَذْنَا أَهْلَهَا بِالْبَأْسَاءِ وَالضَّرَّاءِ ) (٣). دل ذلك أنه يعاملهم بمختلف الأحوال(4) على ما يرى الأصلح لهم والأدعى إلى الحق، وربما يوضحون هذا بمن أنجز ضيافة لرجل وأمره بحضوره وأراد ذلك، وعلم أنه لو دعاه ببشر وملايته لحضر وحصل مراده، ولو فعل ذلك بغلظة وعبوس لم يحضر ، يجب عليه أن يدعوه ببشر وملاطفة ولو عامله بضد ذلك لما كان حكيمًا، وصار منع ذلك بمنزلة منع التمكين من الحضور، ومنع التمكين عن فعل ما أمره به وذا ليس بحكمة بل هو سفه فكذا (٥) هذا ، وربما يقولون لو أعطى العبد ما في مقدوره من اللطف انتفع به ولم يتضرر الله تعالى ولو
The sophism (shubhah) of the Mu'tazilites regarding their statement of al-aslah: Their sophism upon which they rely is that we find the wise person, when he commands an act of obedience, loves it and desires it, it is not permissible for him to withhold from the commanded that by which he can attain his obedience, if he is able to give it to him, and his giving it does not remove him from deserving the description of wisdom, and his withholding it does not benefit him (1). Likewise, if he has an enemy who calls him to his allegiance and he must return to his obedience, it is not permissible for him to treat him with harshness or gentleness except with what he knows is most effective in what he wants from him, and most conducive to him to abandon his enmity. If there occur to him two matters, severity and harshness and leniency and gentleness, and he knows that one of them is more conducive to his enemy's return and repentance, and the other is less, so he does the lesser and refrains from doing the more beneficial and more conducive—while both are within his power, such that their giving harms him not and their withholding benefits him not—he would be, in the view of all wise people, blameworthy, outside of deserving the description of generosity and wisdom. Since this is as we have described, and Allah, Mighty and Majestic, is powerful, merciful, generous, knowing the places of need of His servants, commanding them to obey Him and abandon enmity and return to His allegiance—giving does not harm Him nor does withholding benefit Him, nor does any blame befall Him—we know that He does not do with them except the most beneficial (aslah) of things for them in their religion and the most conducive to His obedience, whether that be sickness or health, pleasure or pain, whether they believe or disbelieve, obey or disobey. Allah the Exalted said: 'And We tested them with good and evil that perhaps they would return' (2) (7:168), and He said: 'And We did not send to any city a prophet except that We seized its people with adversity and hardship' (3) (7:94). That indicates that He treats them with varying conditions (4) according to what He sees as most beneficial (aslah) for them and most conducive to the truth. And perhaps they clarify this with the example of one who hosts a man and orders him to attend, and desires that, and knows that if he invites him with cheerfulness and gentleness he would attend and achieve his purpose, but if he does that with harshness and frowning he would not attend—he must invite him with cheerfulness and gentleness, and if he treated him with the opposite, he would not be wise. And refraining from that is like refraining from enabling attendance, and refraining from enabling him to do what he commanded him to do; that is not wisdom but foolishness. So too this (5). And perhaps they say: If the servant is given what is within his power of grace (lutf), he benefits by it, and Allah the Exalted is not harmed, and...
Source pages p. 996

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite argument (shubhah) for the doctrine of al-aslah, that God must do the most beneficial for His servants. The argument begins with an analogy from human conduct: a wise commander who commands an act of obedience and desires it must provide the means for the commanded to obey, provided he is able and it does not harm his wisdom. Similarly, a person dealing with an enemy who seeks reconciliation must treat him in the way most conducive to his return. If given a choice between two approaches, one more effective and the other less, and both are equally within his power, it would be blameworthy to choose the less effective. Applying this to God—who is powerful, merciful, generous, and knows His servants' needs—He must do the most beneficial for them in religion, whether through sickness or health, etc. The argument is supported by Quranic verses (7:168, 7:94) which show that God treats people with varying conditions to bring them back. They further illustrate with the example of a host who invites a guest: if he knows a cheerful invitation would bring the guest, he must use that; otherwise he is foolish. Thus, God must provide the means (lutf) for obedience.

Key Terms
الشبهة (shubhah)
الأصلح (al-aslah)
الحكيم (hakim)
التمكين (tamkin)
اللطف (lutf)
الضرورة (darurah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilite argument for al-aslah is directly applicable to God without qualification.
Correction: The analogy with human wisdom does not necessarily apply to God in all respects, as divine wisdom may transcend human understanding and obligations.

Checkpoint
In the Mu'tazilite argument for al-aslah, what is the key analogy used?
A) A king and his subjects.
B) A wise commander and a host inviting a guest.
C) A father and his son.
D) A teacher and student.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites argued that God's wisdom obligates Him to provide the most beneficial for His servants, drawing an analogy from human conduct and Quranic verses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial that feels harmful, do you truly believe God is doing the most beneficial for you, even if your mind cannot see how? Or do you secretly expect Him to act like a human host who must please you? What shubhah (specious reasoning) might be hiding in your heart, making you resent His choice? Will you today surrender your definition of 'beneficial' to His wisdom?
?
The Mu'tazilites argued that God must provide grace (lutf) to ensure obedience. But what if His grace to you comes as a painful lesson that strips away your self-reliance? Have you ever rejected a hardship, demanding God treat you 'better'? How would your life change if you thanked Him for every trial as the exact lutf your soul needed?
?
You just saw how human analogies can mislead us about God. In what area of your life have you subconsciously expected God to follow human logic—e.g., rewarding good deeds immediately, or never testing sincere believers? Will you now ask Him to purify your understanding of His wisdom, even if it shatters your comfortable expectations?

The Mu'tazili Argument on Child's Death and Its Contradiction
Al-Nasafi presents the Mu'tazili argument and begins refuting it using their own principle of al-aṣlaḥ.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili claim that God kills a child for others' benefit and how Al-Nasafi refutes it by showing it contradicts their own principle of the most beneficial.

Core Idea
Killing a child for others' benefit violates the Mu'tazili own standard of justice.

The Mu'tazila argue that God may cause a child's death in infancy to prevent future harm to others. Al-Nasafi responds by exposing an internal contradiction: according to Mu'tazili principles, this action withholds the child's ultimate benefit (the highest station in Paradise) and thus makes God unjust. Let's examine their claim and the first part of the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazili claim
Carefully read the first sentence where the Mu'tazili argument is presented. Note that they assert God kills the child because He knows the child would cause harm if he grew up.
This is a theological claim about divine foreknowledge and action.
2
Identify the refutation's starting point
Notice how Al-Nasafi begins his response: he calls the claim an 'admission' that God prevents the child's highest benefit. Identify what that highest benefit is.
'Exposure to the highest of the two stations' refers to the opportunity to earn Paradise through free will.
3
Understand the principle of 'most beneficial'
Al-Nasafi invokes the Mu'tazili doctrine that God must always do what is most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ) for each individual, unless doing so would undermine His wisdom. See how he applies this to the child's case.
The Mu'tazila themselves believe God is obligated to give the most beneficial.
4
Grasp the verdict of injustice
Al-Nasafi concludes that by their own criteria, God's action here is unjust because He withholds benefit from an innocent child. This is the core contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعضهم على هذا وقال : إنه أمات الصغير في حال صغره لأنه علم أنه لو بلغ لكفر وأضل غيره وأكفره فأماته في حال صغره لما فيه من مصلحة الغير. قلنا هذا منكم إقرار أنه منع ما هو الأصح للصغير وهو التعريض لأعلى المنزلتين الذي أمهل ذلك البالغ لأجله، إلا أنه منع هذا الأصلح لما فيه من صلاح ذلك الغير، وعندكم منع النفع عن لا جناية له لإصلاح غيره ظلم على هذا. يحققه أن عندكم إنما يجب على الله تعالى بذل الأصلح إذا كان بذله لا يخرجه من استحقاق الوصف بالحكمة، وإماتة هذا الصغير لما فيه من صلاح ذلك الغير منع لمصلحة التعريض لأعلى المنزلتين في حق هذا الصغير، وهذا يخرجه عن استحقاق الوصف بالحكمة فينبغي أن لا يفوت على هذا الصغير مصلحة التعريض لأعلى المنزلتين لمكان مصلحة الصغير، ومن الحاصل أنتم بين طرفي نقيض فإنكم إن قلتم : إماتته في الصغر مصلحة لذلك الكبير فهو مفسدة ولو لم يمته حتى بلغ فله فيه مصلحة التعريض ومفسدة لذلك الغير، فلم يخل كيف ما كان عن مفسدة وترك مصلحة ، ثم هل كان الله تعالى علم أن ماني وزرادشت ومزدك ومسيلمة والمقنع وبابك وقبل ذلك فرعون لو بلغوا لكفروا وأكفروا الناس؟ فإن قالوا : لا . كفروا لتجهيلهم ربهم، وإن قالوا: نعم. قلنا: فلم أمهلهم الله تعالى وقد علم أنهم يكفرون ويضلون الناس وهلا أماتهم مصلحة للناس، وهذا مما لا اعتراض لهم عليه
Some of them said regarding this: He caused the young child to die in his infancy because He knew that if he grew up he would disbelieve, lead others astray, and cause them to disbelieve, so He caused him to die in his infancy for the benefit of others. We say: This is an admission from you that He prevented what is most beneficial for the child—namely, the exposure to the highest of the two stations—for the sake of which He gave respite to that adult. However, He prevented this most beneficial thing because of the benefit of that other. According to you, preventing benefit from one who has no offense for the sake of reforming another is injustice regarding this. Its verification is that according to you, God Most High is only obligated to bestow the most beneficial if its bestowal does not remove Him from being deserving of the attribute of wisdom. The death of this child, because of the benefit of that other, prevents the benefit of exposure to the highest of the two stations with respect to this child, and this removes Him from being deserving of the attribute of wisdom. Therefore, the benefit of exposure to the highest of the two stations should not be missed for this child because of the child's benefit. The result is that you are between two contradictory extremes: if you say that his death in infancy is a benefit for that adult, then it is a harm, and if you do not cause him to die until he grows up, then there is for him the benefit of exposure and harm for that other. So it does not remain, however it may be, free from harm and omission of benefit. Then, did God Most High know that Mani, Zoroaster, Mazdak, Musaylima, al-Muqanna', Babak, and before them Pharaoh—if they had grown up—would disbelieve and cause people to disbelieve? If they say no, they disbelieve by attributing ignorance to their Lord. If they say yes, we say: So why did God Most High give them respite when He knew that they would disbelieve and lead people astray? Why did He not cause them to die as a benefit for the people? And this is something they have no objection to.
Source pages p. 1002

Commentary
This passage is a refutation by Imam al-Nasafi of a Mu'tazili argument regarding God's action in taking the life of a child. The Mu'tazila claim that God causes a child to die in infancy because He knows that if the child grew up, he would become a disbeliever and lead others astray, so He kills him for the benefit of others. Al-Nasafi responds by pointing out that this admission by the Mu'tazila concedes that God withholds the most beneficial thing for the child—namely, the opportunity to reach the highest station in the afterlife (al-ta'rīḍ li-a'lā al-manzilatayn)—for the sake of another's benefit. According to Mu'tazili principles, preventing benefit from someone with no offense is injustice. He then argues that by their own standard, God would only be obligated to give the most beneficial if doing so does not remove Him from deserving the attribute of wisdom. Since the child's death prevents his highest benefit, it would remove God from being wise, which is unacceptable. The author then presents a dilemma: whether the child dies or grows up, there is harm and omission of benefit. Finally, al-Nasafi raises the example of historical figures like Pharaoh, Mani, and others who were allowed to live despite God's foreknowledge of their disbelief and harmful influence, asking why God did not kill them for the benefit of people. This shows inconsistency in the Mu'tazili position.

Key Terms
الأصلح
التعريض لأعلى المنزلتين
الحكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazili argument shows God's compassion by preventing future harm.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows it leads to injustice by denying the child his highest good, contradicting even Mu'tazili principles.

Checkpoint
What does Al-Nasafi identify as the 'most beneficial' thing for the child?
A) Long life
B) Exposure to the highest of the two stations
C) Protection from harm
D) Death in infancy

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili claim that God kills a child for others' benefit contradicts their own principle of al-aṣlaḥ and leads to injustice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see an innocent person suffer or die young, do you ever find yourself questioning God's wisdom or justice? How does this passage challenge you to trust that God's wisdom is not limited by your own calculations of 'benefit'? What would it take for you to surrender that question to God completely?
?
The Mu'tazila attempted to define God's justice by their own rational standard. In your own life, have you ever silently accused God of being unfair because something didn't match your sense of what was 'best'? Will you now repent of that and submit to His inscrutable wisdom, even when it hurts?
?
The child in the argument lost the highest station for the benefit of others. Reflect: has God ever withheld something precious from you—perhaps a desire, a comfort, or a blessing—for the sake of someone else's good? Can you accept that as just? If not, what does your resistance reveal about your perception of God's sovereignty and your own entitlement?

The Inescapable Dilemma and the Historical Proof
Al-Nasafi presents a dilemma and a historical example to complete his refutation of the Mu'tazili argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will see how Al-Nasafi presents a dilemma that every outcome involves harm, and then uses the example of historical disbelievers to show the Mu'tazili position is absurd.

Core Idea
Whether the child lives or dies, there is always harm—and God lets some disbelievers live.

Al-Nasafi continues his refutation by showing the Mu'tazila are caught between two extremes: if the child dies, it's a harm to him; if he lives, it's a harm to others. Then he asks a devastating question: why didn't God kill historically known tyrants like Pharaoh and Mani? This forces the Mu'tazila into a contradiction. Let's explore the second part of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma
Read the part beginning with 'The result is that you are between two contradictory extremes'. Notice that whether the child dies or grows up, there is both harm and omission of benefit.
This shows that the Mu'tazili position cannot avoid negative consequences.
2
Analyze the two horns
First horn: if the child dies, it's a benefit for the adult but harm for the child (loss of his benefit). Second horn: if he lives, the child gains benefit but the adult is harmed. So no option is purely beneficial.
3
Consider the historical challenge
Al-Nasafi asks about famous disbelievers (Pharaoh, Mani, etc.) whom God allowed to live despite knowing they would cause immense harm. If the Mu'tazili logic were correct, God should have killed them in infancy.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Recognize the forced conclusion
If the Mu'tazila say God did not know, they attribute ignorance to God (which they reject). If they say He knew, then why didn't He kill them? They have no answer, so their argument collapses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

The Mu'tazili Contradiction: Tamkīn and Luṭf
Al-Nasafi exposes the Mu'tazili contradiction between tamkīn and luṭf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the internal contradiction between the Mu'tazili concepts of tamkīn (empowerment) and luṭf (grace) as exposed by al-Nasafi.

Core Idea
If tamkīn suffices for action, luṭf is redundant; if luṭf is needed, tamkīn is insufficient.

Al-Nasafi points out a key inconsistency in the Mu'tazili theory of human action. They maintain that a morally responsible person must have tamkīn (empowerment) to act, yet they also posit an additional divine grace (luṭf) that is necessary for making a choice. This passage shows how these two claims conflict, undermining their own framework of human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic Passage
Read the original Arabic text once to get the flow of al-Nasafi's argument. Then read the literal translation to ensure understanding.
Focus on the sequence: tamkīn claim → luṭf definition.
2
Identify the Two Mu'tazili Claims
The passage states that the Mu'tazila require tamkīn for action, and then they add luṭf as an additional factor. Identify how they define luṭf.
Notice the phrase 'thumma warā'a l-tamkīn wa-l-qudra maʿānī' — beyond empowerment and capability are meanings.
3
Understand the Contradiction
Ask yourself: if tamkīn alone suffices to reach the act, why is luṭf needed? And if luṭf is needed, then tamkīn cannot be sufficient. This is the contradiction al-Nasafi exposes.
The contradiction is egregious (fāḥish) because it violates logical coherence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لهم تناقض فاحش فإنهم زعموا أن المكلف لا بد له من التمكن من الفعل وبثبوت التمكن يتوصل إلى الفعل، ثم وراء التمكن والقدرة معاني ويسمونها لطفا، ويفسرون اللطف أنه ما يختار عنده المكلف ما كلف من أخذ وترك لولاه لكان لا يختار أو يكون أقرب إلى هذا الاختيار فما هذا حاله يسمونه لطفا
Then they have an egregious contradiction: they claimed that the morally responsible person (al-mukallaf) necessarily requires empowerment (al-tamkīn) to act, and by the establishment of empowerment he reaches the act. Then, beyond empowerment and capability (al-qudra), there are meanings which they call grace (luṭf), and they interpret grace as that by which the morally responsible person chooses what he is commanded—of taking and leaving—without which he would not choose, or it brings him closer to this choice. So that which is in this state they call grace.
Source pages p. 1002

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of a contradiction in the Mu'tazili doctrine concerning human action. Al-Nasafi states that the Mu'tazila hold that the morally responsible person (al-mukallaf) must first have empowerment (tamkīn) to perform an act, and through empowerment he can perform the act. However, they also affirm that beyond empowerment and capability (qudra) there are additional meanings which they call luṭf (divine grace). They define luṭf as something that causes the servant to choose what he is commanded (whether to do or refrain), such that without it he would not choose, or it brings him closer to that choice. Al-Nasafi points out the contradiction: if empowerment alone suffices for action, then luṭf is redundant; if luṭf is needed, then empowerment is not sufficient, which undermines their doctrine of human free will and responsibility.

Key Terms
التمكن
القدرة
اللطف
الاختيار
المكلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazili concept of luṭf is similar to predestination or divine coercion.
Correction: The passage shows luṭf is meant to aid choice, but by making it necessary, it contradicts their emphasis on human empowerment (tamkīn), not that it forces action.

Checkpoint
According to the text, how do the Mu'tazila define luṭf (grace)?
A) It is the power to act.
B) It is what helps the servant to choose what he is commanded, without which he would not choose or brings him closer to choice.
C) It is God's eternal will.
D) It is the opposite of tamkīn.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili doctrines of tamkīn and luṭf are internally contradictory because they cannot both be necessary for human action without undermining each other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if your own ability (tamkīn) is sufficient for every good deed, or do you humbly acknowledge that without Allah's grace (luṭf) you would not choose what is right? What would change if you truly recognized your need for His help in every moral choice?
?
When you fall short in obedience, do you rely on your own resolve to improve, or do you turn to Allah for the luṭf that alone makes you choose rightly? How does the Mu'tazili contradiction exposed in this passage challenge any self-sufficiency in your heart?
?
Is there an area of your life where you claim full responsibility for your actions, yet secretly hope for divine grace when you struggle? This passage shows that such a position is contradictory. Will you today admit your dependence on Allah's grace in all your choices and ask Him for it?

Tamkin and Lutf in Mu'tazilite Theology
Explains Mu'tazilite concepts of tamkin and lutf, with views of Abu Hashim and al-Jubba'i.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite concepts of tamkin (enabling) and lutf (grace), and the debate between Abu Hashim and al-Jubba'i regarding the source and obligation of lutf.

Core Idea
Tamkin is minimal ability; lutf is extra assistance that tips the scales toward obedience.

The Mu'tazilites developed a sophisticated theory of divine obligation that required two elements: tamkin (enabling) and lutf (grace). The first is the basic capacity to act, while the second is additional circumstantial help that inclines the servant to choose obedience. This passage from the Tabṣirah outlines these concepts and records a key difference between Abu Hashim and al-Jubba'i on whether lutf must come only from God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the flow.
Notice the technical terms tamkin and lutf.
2
Identify the definitions
Locate how the passage defines tamkin and lutf. What is the difference between them?
Tamkin is necessary; lutf is not necessary but facilitates.
3
Analyze the examples
Examine the examples given for lutf: illnesses, wealth, admonitions. How do they influence choice?
These states affect motives and thus count as lutfs.
4
Compare the two scholars' views
Contrast Abu Hashim's and al-Jubba'i's positions on the source of lutf and whether God is obligated to provide it.
Abu Hashim allows lutf from others; al-Jubba'i restricts it to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويزعمون أن الله تعالى إذا كلف عبدًا لا بد له من أمرين: أحدهما ما يتمكن به والآخر ما عنده يختار أو يقوى اختيار فالأول يسمى تمكينًا، وهو الذي لا بد منه في فعل ما كلف. والثاني: يسمى لطفا لأن الفعل قد يصح على الوجه الذي كلف دونه لكنه إذا كان حصل يصير حاله في دواعيه بخلاف حاله إذا لم يكن، وذلك كالأمراض والمصائب والغموم والفقر والغنى وغير ذلك من الأسباب، فإن الإنسان قد يختار عند حالة من هذه الأحوال ما لا يختار عند غيرها فكانت هذه الأحوال المتفرقة في لطف كل واحد منهم ما اختص به من الحالة. ثم عند أبي هاشم قد يكون اللطف من الله تعالى وقد يجوز أن يكون من غير الله تعالى، فإنه إذا كان في معلوم الله تعالى أن رزق إنسانًا إذا اتسع عليه كان أقرب إلى طاعته يوسع عليه الرزق ثم قد يكون ذلك بالهبة والوصية، وهما فعل غير الله تعالى ولا تفاوت في حق اللطف بين هذا وبين ما يوصل إليه الله تعالى، وكذا قد يحصل ذلك بالتنبيه والوعظ والتذكير من الصالحين. وقال الجبائي: لا يكون اللطف من قبل غير الله تعالى في تكليف المكلف إذ لو كان لما وجب على الكل الفزع في الألطاف إليه تعالى، ثم عند أبي هاشم ما كان من الألطاف من فعل الله تعالى يجب على الله تعالى تحصيله وما كان من فعل غير الله فليس بواجب تحصيله لكن التكليف به كان معلقا فإن كان في المعلوم حصوله كلف الله تعالى وإلا لم يكلف هذا هو تقرير مذاهبهم في اللطف.
They claim that when God Most High obligates a servant, he must have two things: one by which he is enabled (tamkin), and the other by which he chooses or his choice is strengthened. The first is called tamkin, which is necessary for performing what he is obligated to do. The second is called lutf (grace), because the act may be valid in the manner he is obligated without it, but when it occurs, his state regarding his motives becomes different from what it would be if it did not occur. Such states are like illnesses, calamities, sorrows, poverty, wealth, and other causes. For a man may choose in one of these states what he would not choose in another, so these varying states each constitute a lutf for the individual in accordance with his specific state. Then according to Abu Hashim, lutf may come from God Most High or may possibly come from other than God. For if it is in God's knowledge that providing for a person abundantly would make him closer to obedience, He expands his provision. This may occur through a gift or bequest, both actions of other than God, and there is no difference in respect of lutf between this and what God conveys to him. Similarly, this may occur through the admonition, preaching, and reminder of righteous people. Al-Jubba'i said: Lutf does not come from other than God in obligating the obligated, because if it did, everyone would be required to resort to God for the lutfs. Then according to Abu Hashim, that which is from God's actions of lutfs must be procured by God Most High, while that from other than God is not obligatory to procure, but the obligation regarding it is suspended: if it is known that it will occur, God obligates; otherwise He does not obligate. This is the exposition of their schools concerning lutf.
Source pages p. 1004

Commentary
This passage explains the Mu'tazilite doctrine of tamkin and lutf. The author first states that according to the Mu'tazilites, when God obligates a servant, two things are necessary: tamkin (enabling) and lutf (grace). Tamkin is defined as the minimal ability required for performing the obligated act. Lutf is defined as additional circumstances that make the act easier and incline the servant towards choice. Examples include health, wealth, poverty, or admonitions that affect one's motives. The author then reports a difference between Abu Hashim and al-Jubba'i. Abu Hashim holds that lutf can come from God or from other beings (e.g., gifts from people), and that when it is from God, God is obligated to provide it. Al-Jubba'i restricts lutf to God alone, arguing that otherwise all would be obliged to seek lutfs from God. Abu Hashim distinguishes obligatory lutfs (from God) from non-obligatory ones (from others), and says obligation is conditional on God's knowledge of its occurrence. The passage concludes by stating this is a summary of their views.

Key Terms
تمكين
لطف
أبو هاشم
الجبائي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that tamkin and lutf are the same thing or that lutf is necessary for an act to be valid.
Correction: Tamkin is the minimal ability required for any obligated act; lutf is an additional facilitator that makes obedience easier but is not strictly necessary for the act's validity.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilites, what is the primary difference between tamkin and lutf?
A) Tamkin is from God, lutf is from humans.
B) Tamkin is necessary for obligation, lutf is not necessary but facilitates choice.
C) Tamkin is for actions, lutf is for beliefs.
D) Tamkin and lutf are identical.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites distinguish between tamkin (enabling ability) and lutf (grace), with disagreement among their theologians about the source and obligatoriness of lutf.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when a sudden change—illness, wealth, or a word from a friend—completely shifted your choices toward obedience or away from it. The Mu'tazilites call that circumstance 'lutf.' Do you see God's hand in that, or do you credit only the human cause? Examine your heart: are you quick to attribute your obedience to your own strength and your disobedience to your circumstances?
?
Abu Hashim teaches that lutf can come through other people—a gift, a bequest, or a reminder. Who in your life has been such a lutf for you? Have you ever thanked either God or that person? Now ask yourself: are you being a lutf for others? Whom might God be calling you to admonish or help today, not to judge them, but to tip their hearts toward Him?
?
You believe you have tamkin—the basic ability to do what God asks. But do you actually live as if you do? Or do you hide behind excuses like 'I'm weak' or 'I can't'? What if the real lack is not ability but the will to choose obedience? What would it cost you to stop excusing yourself and to ask God for the lutf to make the hard choice—starting today?

The Internal Contradiction in Mu'tazilite Lutf Doctrine
Critiques the Mu'tazilite view on lutf, showing the reward paradox.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite claim that lutf is necessary for action contradicts their own assumption about reward and tamkin.

Core Idea
If a harder act brings more reward, withholding lutf is more beneficial.

The Mu'tazilites argued that God must provide lutf (grace) to enable obedience. The author counters that acts can be performed without lutf, and doing so yields greater reward. This raises a dilemma: if God withholds lutf, the servant gains a higher reward; if He grants it, the reward is lower. The Mu'tazilite position thus leads to an absurd conclusion about divine wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite claim
Recall that Mu'tazilites believe God must provide lutf to enable acts of obedience, otherwise He would be unjust.
2
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the structure: first the contradiction, then the argument about tamkin and reward.
3
Analyze the reward logic
The author says performing an act without lutf is harder and more rewarding. So why would God grant lutf and reduce reward? This exposes inconsistency in Mu'tazilite reasoning.
The phrase 'أعلى المنزلتين' refers to the higher station of reward.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Even if God knows the servant won't act without lutf, withholding it still exposes him to higher reward, while granting it lowers him. Thus the Mu'tazilite position is self-defeating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم هذا منهم مناقضة عظيمة حيث أرجعوا اللطف على الله تعالى، وذلك أن الفعل يتصور حصوله بالتمكن بدون هذا اللطف على ما حكينا، ولا شك أن تحصيل الفعل بدون اللطف أشق على البدن وأكثر ثوابًا، وتحصيله مع اللطف أخف وأيسر على البدن فيكون أقل ثوابًا فكان الأصلح للعبد والتعويض لأعلى المنزلتين أن يمنعه اللطف ليكون ثواب فعله أجزل والمنفعة به أوفر وأكمل، وإن كان يعلم الله تعالى أنه لا يفعل بدون اللطف ولكن مع هذا منعه تعريض لأعلى المنزلتين وإعطائه إزالة له على أعلى المنزلتين إلى أدونهما، وهذا ليس يصلح كما أنه تعالى يمهل من يعلم أنه لو بلغ لكفر ومن يعلم أنه بعد ما زجى عمره في الإسلام ثمانين سنة يرتد بعد ذلك إذ هو بهذا الإمهال عرضة لأعلى المنزلتين، وإن كان يعلم أنه يكفر ويرتد فإن كانت العبرة لما يحصل من العاقبة المعلومة، وكان إعطاء اللطف أصلح إذ لو لم يعطه لما فعل لكان ينبغي أن لا يمهل هناك فإنه إن كان لا يمهل لا يكفر، وسقط التعريض على مصلحة يعلم أنها لا تحصل في الفصلين جميعًا، وإن كانت العبرة لنفس المصلحة وكان التعريض لها هو الأصلح، ولا عبرة للعلم بحصولها وعدم حصولها، كان منع اللطف هاهنا والتعريض لأعلى المنزلتين أصلح فأما اعتبار العلم في باب إعطاء اللطف وإسقاط اعتباره في باب الإمهال إلى أن يكفر فبناء للأمر على تشهي النفس وميلان الهوى دون الدليل والعقل عصمنا الله تعالى عن ذلك.
Then this is a great contradiction from them, since they attributed lutf to God Most High. That is because the act is conceivable without this lutf, by virtue of tamkin alone, as we have related. There is no doubt that performing the act without lutf is harder on the body and brings greater reward, while performing it with lutf is lighter and easier on the body, thus less rewarding. Therefore, the more beneficial for the servant and the compensation for the higher of the two stations would be to withhold lutf from him, so that the reward for his act be greater and the benefit more abundant and complete. Even if God Most High knows that he will not act without lutf, nevertheless withholding it exposes him to the higher station, while granting it lowers him from the higher station to the lower. This is not proper. For He also grants respite to one whom He knows would disbelieve if he reached a certain point, and to one whom He knows that after spending eighty years in Islam he would afterwards apostatize, for by this respite he is exposed to the higher station. Even if He knows that he will disbelieve and apostatize, then if consideration is given to the outcome that occurs, and granting lutf is more beneficial because if it were not granted he would not act, then He should not grant respite there, for if He does not grant respite, he would not disbelieve, and the exposure to a benefit that is known not to occur would be lost in both cases. If consideration is given to the benefit itself and exposure to it is the more beneficial, and knowledge of its occurrence or non-occurrence is irrelevant, then withholding lutf here and exposing to the higher station is more beneficial. As for considering knowledge in the matter of granting lutf while disregarding it in the matter of granting respite until apostasy, that is built on whim and caprice, not on evidence and reason. May God protect us from that.
Source pages p. 1004

Commentary
This passage is a refutation (naqd) of the Mu'tazilite doctrine of lutf. The author argues that the Mu'tazilite position is self-contradictory. First, he states that acts can be performed with tamkin alone, without lutf. So lutf is not strictly necessary. If an act is done without lutf, it is harder and brings more reward; with lutf, it is easier and less rewarding. Therefore, for the servant's ultimate benefit, it would be better for God to withhold lutf so the servant can attain the higher reward. Even if God knows the servant will not act without lutf, withholding it still exposes him to the possibility of higher reward, while granting it guarantees only a lower reward. The author then draws an analogy with God granting respite to a person He knows will eventually disbelieve: in that case, the Mu'tazilites do not deny respite, yet they apply a different standard to lutf. The author analyzes two possible principles: either consideration is given to the known outcome, or to the benefit of exposure itself. If the first, then God should not grant respite to the eventual disbeliever (since He knows the bad outcome), but He does. If the second, then withholding lutf is more beneficial because it exposes to higher reward. The Mu'tazilites are inconsistent: they apply knowledge to lutf but not to respite. This shows their position is based on desire, not reason. The author concludes by seeking God's protection from such error.

Key Terms
مناقضة
تمكن
ثواب
تعريض
أعلى المنزلتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that if God knows a person will not obey without lutf, then granting lutf is necessary for justice.
Correction: The author shows that even then, withholding lutf exposes the person to higher reward, which is better for them, so God's wisdom would favor withholding.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is performing an act without lutf more rewarding?
A) Because it is harder on the body
B) Because it shows more faith
C) Because God loves difficulty
D) Because it is done with tamkin only

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite claim that God must grant lutf leads to a contradiction because withholding lutf would result in a higher reward for the servant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself seeking ease in worship, do you ever consider that you might be choosing a lesser reward? Will you embrace difficulty in obedience today for the sake of a greater thawab?
?
If God withholds ease from you in a certain act of worship, do you complain or trust that He is exposing you to a higher station? What specific trial can you reframe as divine ta'rid to a greater reward?
?
Are you willing to risk disobedience (by not having lutf) for the chance of a higher rank, or do you prefer the guaranteed but lower reward of ease? Which choice dominates your heart right now, and what will you do about it?

The Inconsistency in Mu'tazilite Use of Divine Knowledge
Critiques Mu'tazilite inconsistency between lutf and imhal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author exposes the Mu'tazilites' inconsistent application of God's foreknowledge in their doctrines of lutf and imhal (respite).

Core Idea
Mu'tazilites apply divine knowledge to lutf but ignore it for respite, showing whim, not reason.

The author extends his refutation by drawing an analogy with God's granting of respite (imhal) to people He knows will eventually disbelieve. The Mu'tazilites do not object to this, yet they apply a different standard to lutf. This reveals an inconsistency: they consider divine knowledge when it supports their view, but ignore it otherwise. The author concludes that their position is based on desire, not evidence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the respite analogy
God grants respite to people He knows will disbelieve later. This exposes them to higher reward if they reform, but God knows they won't.
The analogy parallels the lutf case where God knows the servant won't act without lutf.
2
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. The author considers two principles: considering the outcome vs. considering the benefit of exposure itself.
3
Analyze the two possibilities
If outcome matters, then God should not grant respite to the eventual disbeliever (since He knows the bad outcome). But He does. If exposure itself matters, then withholding lutf is better. Either way, Mu'tazilites are inconsistent.
4
Identify the inconsistency
Mu'tazilites apply God's knowledge when granting lutf but not when granting respite. This shows their doctrine is built on whim, not consistent reasoning.

Refutation of the Mu'tazilite Argument on Divine Grace (Lutf)
Refutes the Mu'tazilite position that God must grant lutf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Maturidi school argues that withholding divine grace (lutf) can be more beneficial for the servant's reward than granting it.

Core Idea
Withholding grace can be more merciful than granting it.

The Mu'tazilites claimed that God is obliged to grant grace (lutf) to enable obedience. Imam al-Nasafi refutes this by showing that performing acts without grace is harder but yields greater reward, so withholding grace is actually more beneficial. In this passage, he exposes the internal contradiction in the Mu'tazilite position.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Note the key term 'لطف' (lutf) and its context.
2
Understand the core argument
The author argues that an act can be performed without grace; doing so is harder but earns greater reward. Thus, withholding grace is more beneficial for the servant.
Consider the logic: harder act → more reward → better for the servant.
3
Analyze the analogy with respite
The author compares withholding grace to giving respite to a potential disbeliever. Both expose the servant to the highest station, even if the outcome is known.
Why does this analogy challenge the Mu'tazilite position?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا منهم مناقضة عظيمة حيث أرجعوا اللطف على الله تعالى، وذلك أن الفعل يتصور حصوله بالتمكن بدون هذا اللطف على ما حكينا، ولا شك أن تحصيل الفعل بدون اللطف أشق على البدن وأكثر ثوابًا، وتحصيله مع اللطف أخف وأيسر على البدن فيكون أقل ثوابًا فكان الأصلح للعبد [؟] والتعويض لأعلى المنزلتين أن يمنعه اللطف ليكون ثواب فعله أجزل والمنفعة به أوفر وأكمل، وإن كان يعلم الله تعالى أنه لا يفعل بدون اللطف ولكن مع هذا منعه تعريض لأعلى المنزلتين وإعطائه إزالة له على أعلى المنزلتين إلى أدونهما، وهذا ليس يصلح كما أنه تعالى يمهل من يعلم أنه لو بلغ لكفر ومن يعلم أنه بعد ما زجى عمره في الإسلام ثمانين سنة يرتد بعد ذلك إذ هو بهذا الإمهال عرضة لأعلى المنزلتين، وإن كان يعلم أنه يكفر ويرتد فإن كانت العبرة لما يحصل من العاقبة المعلومة، وكان إعطاء اللطف أصلح إذ لو لم يعطه لما فعل لكان ينبغي أن لا يمهل هناك فإنه إن كان لا يمهل لا يكفر، وسقط التعريض على مصلحة يعلم أنها لا تحصل في الفصلين جميعًا، وإن كانت العبرة لنفس المصلحة وكان التعريض لها هو الأصلح، ولا عبرة للعلم بحصولها وعدم حصولها، كان منع اللطف هاهنا والتعريض لأعلى المنزلتين أصلح فأما اعتبار العلم في باب إعطاء اللطف وإسقاط اعتباره في باب الإمهال إلى أن يكفر فبناء للأمر على تشهي النفس وميلان الهوى دون الدليل والعقل عصمنا الله تعالى عن ذلك.
This is a great contradiction from them where they attributed grace (al-lutf) to God Almighty. That is because the act is conceivable to occur with capability without this grace according to what we have related. There is no doubt that performing the act without grace is harder on the body and greater in reward, while performing it with grace is lighter and easier on the body, thus it is lesser in reward. So the most beneficial for the servant and the compensation for the highest of the two stations is that He withholds grace from him so that the reward of his act is more abundant and the benefit is more ample and complete. And if God Almighty knows that he will not act without grace, nevertheless withholding it from him is an exposure to the highest station, and granting it is a removal of him from the highest station to the lower one. This is not proper, just as He Almighty gives respite to one whom He knows that if he reached [a certain age] he would disbelieve, and to one whom He knows that after spending eighty years in Islam he will apostatize afterwards. For by this respite he is exposed to the highest station, even though He knows he will disbelieve and apostatize. If the consideration is for what results from the known outcome, and granting grace is more beneficial since if He had not granted it he would not have acted, then it would follow that He should not give respite there; for if He does not give respite, he will not disbelieve, and the exposure to a benefit that is known not to occur falls away in both cases. And if the consideration is for the benefit itself and exposure to it is more beneficial, and there is no regard for knowledge of its occurrence or non-occurrence, then withholding grace here and exposure to the highest station is more beneficial. As for considering knowledge in the matter of granting grace and disregarding it in the matter of giving respite until he disbelieves, it is an ordering based on the desire of the soul and the inclination of caprice without proof and reason. May God Almighty protect us from that.
Source pages p. 1005

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that God is obliged to grant grace (lutf) to enable human beings to perform acts of obedience. The author begins by stating that this position involves a great contradiction. He argues that an act can be performed without divine grace; performing it without grace is harder but earns greater reward, while performing it with grace is easier but earns lesser reward. Therefore, it is actually more beneficial for the servant and a form of compensation for the highest station (i.e., the highest rank in Paradise) for God to withhold grace, so that the servant's reward is greater. Even if God knows that the servant will not act without grace, withholding grace still exposes him to the possibility of attaining the highest station, whereas granting grace would move him to a lower station. The author draws an analogy with God's giving respite to those He knows will disbelieve or apostatize: by granting time, He exposes them to the highest station, even though He knows the outcome. If the criterion were the known outcome, then granting grace would be considered more beneficial, but that would imply God should not give respite at all—which is absurd. If the criterion is the benefit itself regardless of knowledge, then withholding grace is more beneficial. Mixing these criteria is arbitrary and based on desire, not reason.

Key Terms
لطف
ثواب
تعريض
منزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that divine grace (lutf) is always beneficial for the servant and that God is obliged to grant it.
Correction: The author shows that withholding grace can be more beneficial because it leads to greater reward through harder effort, and that God is not obliged to grant it based on the Mu'tazilite reasoning.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is it more beneficial for God to withhold grace (lutf) from the servant?
A) Because the servant does not need grace to perform acts.
B) Because performing acts without grace is harder and yields greater reward.
C) Because God does not know whether the servant will obey.
D) Because the servant can never obey without grace.

Key Takeaway
Withholding divine grace can be more beneficial for the servant because it leads to greater reward through harder effort, challenging the Mu'tazilite claim that God must grant grace.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find an act of worship difficult and feel no divine ease, do you become frustrated or do you recognize it as an opportunity for greater reward? Will you embrace difficulty as a gift from today?
?
God gives respite even to those He knows will disbelieve. Are you using the time and ease He gives you to draw nearer to Him, or are you taking it for granted? What specific change will you make in your daily routine to respond to His respite with gratitude and obedience?
?
The Mu'tazilites tried to impose their own reasoning on God. Are there areas in your life where you insist on how God should treat you rather than submitting to His wisdom? Which aspect of your relationship with God do you need to surrender to His decree today?
